### Post by s.fox on 2011-11-22
The intention of this thread is for the community to provide constructive comments on Unity.

This thread nor the forums as a whole are not the correct place to complain about either Unity or the depreciation of Gnome 2. Gnome decided to discontinue Gnome 2, you can contact them [here]("http://www.gnome.org/contact/").

Ubuntu developers do not read these forums, you are encouraged to [report bugs]("https://help.ubuntu.com/community/ReportingBugs") on launchpad.

We will not allow ranting.  Such posts will be jailed.  All posts are subject to the [Code of Conduct]("http://ubuntuforums.org/index.php?page=policy").

Here are some key points that the forum staff wish to pass on:
> 
1. Gnome 2 is no longer supported by Gnome. If you want gnome 2 use [Scientific Linux]("http://www.scientificlinux.org/") or [Debian Stable]("http://www.debian.org/").

2. Unity is the Ubuntu default.

3. Using Ubuntu does not mean that you have to use Unity. You may try one of the many other desktop environments that Ubuntu offers in their repositories including but not limited to [Gnome-shell]("http://live.gnome.org/GnomeShell"), [Openbox]("http://openbox.org/wiki/Openbox:Screenshots"), [Fluxbox]("http://fluxbox.org/screenshots/"); and [Enlightenment]("http://enlightenment.org/"). Or you may wish to try one of the official Ubuntu derivatives [Kubuntu]("http://www.kubuntu.org/"), [Xubuntu]("http://www.xubuntu.org/") or [Lubuntu]("http://lubuntu.net/").

4. Be constructive.  If you do not like something in Unity get involved with development.  Look [here]("http://unity.ubuntu.com/getinvolved/") for how to do so.

5. 'Classic gnome' can be enabled by following [this]("http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=1859961") guide.Finally the forum staff will not be merging support threads into this thread. This thread is not for support.

This post may be updated periodically by forum staff, so please do check back.

---

### Post by rg4w on 2011-11-22
Good move starting this thread, s.fox.  Here's my first contribution:

OMG! CCSM!

Here's the deal:  Ever since Unity came out I kinda liked it but wanted to see some refinement in a few small but key areas, specifically:

- Drop the background color behind the icons in the Launcher, as it makes the icon shapes appear muddy and difficult to discern (for example the Ubuntu Software Center is almost completely washed out)

- Fine-tune the auto-hide behaviors, so I can adjust the delay interval to something that works better for me

- Change the icon size, making it a little smaller so I can get the full benefit of the Launcher but have it take up less room and show more icons

Solution:  Compiz Config Settings Manager.  It lets you do all that and much more.

Okay, this isn't exactly new, and many folks already know how to get it.  But I went many weeks until I found it, so for those of you who want to tweak Unity and haven't yet installed CCSM here's the trick:

In the Ubuntu Software Center, search for Unity.  Unity itself will be the first hit, and when you click "More Info..." you'll see a list of checkboxes for all the components that comprise the package.

At the bottom of that list is one checkbox which is not set by default:  Compiz Config.

Turn it on, let it install, and then open it.

This version is not only compatible with Unity, but sports a new Unity icon in the main panel.  Definitely click that, and enjoy!   Plenty of options there for fine-tuning your Unity experience.

This is old hat for some, but dang! I wished I'd found it sooner, and before I tried this I'd come across some posts that said that CCSM wasn't compatible with Unity.  That may be true for some versions, but the version I installed from the Unity page in USC works great.  Really great.  

At last I have the customized experience that's a big part of what I love about using Linux.

### Post by Jacobonbuntu on 2011-11-22
I already love this thread from the start.....

I grew really quite fond of the Unity Interface. I do believe however that Ubuntu is not presenting itself in the best possible way when just installed. With a few very easy additions/changes many people would have a better first impression, especially those who come from another OS.


[LIST=1]
[*]for me the most important would be that the "home" icon should be a quicklist for all folders in the home directory, with a new-window entry (home). The first encounter is now rather clumsy when there is only one "track' to navigate. I know people say you can use middle-click, but clicking the mouse wheel (which *is* the middle) is not ergonomic. Another content of the .desktop-file would be without any investment.
The "favorite places" -entry in the top panel is useless to navigate; as soon as an application is opened, the top-panel is claimed by the global menu of the application and no longer available for any other functionality than the indicators.
[*]the icons could easily be (much) smaller, I don't know anybody who thinks this is a good size :). Also: I believe the launcher's behaviour should be not to hide by default.
[/LIST]
As I said, for me these are no issues and easy to change, but for newcomers it would make the first impression more comfortable I think

### Post by rg4w on 2011-11-22
> **Olle Wiklund said:**
> I am not happy reading the current 'popularity window' of [[COLOR=RoyalBlue]_http://distrowatch.com_[/COLOR]]("http://distrowatch.com") See the statistics of the last 30 days
Could someone please sticky this? <g>:

DistroWatch stats represent page hit counts at DistroWatch only.  DistroWatch is a site frequented by distro-hoppers, but rarely if ever by the rest of the computing world.  Almost none of my friends, many of whom are long-time computing professionals, have ever even heard of the site.

For comparison, we can look at Alexa site rankings as a useful measure of relative interest:

[microsoft.com]("http://www.alexa.com/siteinfo/microsoft.com"): 28
[apple.com]("http://www.alexa.com/siteinfo/apple.com"): 30
[ubuntu.com]("http://www.alexa.com/siteinfo/ubuntu.com"): 1,194
[linuxmint.com]("http://www.alexa.com/siteinfo/linuxmint.com"): 9,685 
[distrowatch.com]("http://www.alexa.com/siteinfo/distrowatch.com"): 12,773
[ubuntusatanic.org]("http://www.alexa.com/siteinfo/ubuntusatanic.org"): 952,318 :)

In short, DistroWatch numbers bear no relationship at all to any actual measure of installed base or even general interest for the market as a whole.

Moreover, as Ubuntu continues to grow among the general population we can expect its relative ranking at a site that caters specifically to distro switchers to drop, since a large majority those switching to any other distro are likely coming *from Ubuntu*.

These realities of the inherent nature of DistroWatch stats have been discussed time and again, yet they keep coming up.

Aren't any other statistics, any at all, that show Ubuntu popularity in the sort of alarming decline so frequently suggested here?

Meanwhile:

Dell offers machines with Ubuntu Linux in 220 Chinese stores
[http://www.theinquirer.net/inquirer/news/2120655/dell-offers-machines-ubuntu-linux-220-chinese-stores](http://www.theinquirer.net/inquirer/news/2120655/dell-offers-machines-ubuntu-linux-220-chinese-stores)

Ubuntu launches at retail in Portugal with ASUS 
[http://blog.canonical.com/2011/11/18/ubuntu-launches-at-retail-in-portugal-with-asus/](http://blog.canonical.com/2011/11/18/ubuntu-launches-at-retail-in-portugal-with-asus/)

Asus will preload Ubuntu Linux on three Eee PCs
[http://www.theinquirer.net/inquirer/news/2075819/asus-preload-ubuntu-linux-eee-pcs](http://www.theinquirer.net/inquirer/news/2075819/asus-preload-ubuntu-linux-eee-pcs)


Very few people ever choose an OS; they just use whatever came with their computer.

Where are the Mint machines?

Another **attempt** to gauge usage is the inclusion of **Ubuntu** in the browser's useragent string. Firefox 8, if downloaded as an update from Ubuntu (and not direct from Mozilla) has this:
Mozilla/5.0 (**Ubuntu**; X11; Linux i686; rv:8.0) Gecko/20100101 Firefox/8.0

### Post by larjan on 2011-11-22
I'm using 11.04 at the moment. I like the dock and had little trouble figuring out how to configure it to suit me. If, like me, your not nuts about the dash and prefer menus, here's an option that works for me. Install mint menu. I don't know if it will work in 11.10, but it does in 11.04. Like it or not, things are going to touch screen, simply because that is what the majority of people want. If any OS is going to be used by the average person, (who knows little about how things in their computers work, and doesn't care to) in ever increasing numbers, they will have to support this. Desktops are losing in numbers to smaller devices. It stands to reason, if "any" OS is going to be successfull in wide spread use,which garners more support and therefore makes success more likely, they have to support touch screens and smaller screens and people who want icons not text menus. So the way I see it, If I want a different interface than this, at least I am using an OS that allows for this and there are plenty of choices. Bottom line, I don't see a problem.

---

Firefox has removed the OS version from the ueragent string, whereas Chromium still does:

> "Mozilla/5.0 (X11; Linux x86_64) AppleWebKit/535.2 (KHTML, like Gecko) Ubuntu/12.04 Chromium/15.0.874.102 Chrome/15.0.874.102 Safari/535.2"

### Post by vasa1 on 2011-11-23
> **cariboo907 said:**
> Firefox has removed the OS version from the useragent string, whereas Chromium still does:

My guess is that the string has been modified by the Ubuntu team?

The Chrome, not Chromium, that I got from the Google ppa ([http://dl.google.com/linux/chrome/deb/stable](http://dl.google.com/linux/chrome/deb/stable)) doesn't mention Ubuntu:
Mozilla/5.0 (X11; Linux i686) AppleWebKit/535.2 (KHTML, like Gecko) Chrome/15.0.874.121 Safari/535.2

In any case, I followed the instructions [here]("http://johnbokma.com/mexit/2004/04/24/changinguseragent.html") to add **Ubuntu** to the string of an Aurora build I picked up direct from Mozilla.

So now I have```
Mozilla/5.0 (**Ubuntu**; X11; Linux i686; rv:10.0a2) Gecko/20111113 Firefox/10.0a2
```

### Post by Jacobonbuntu on 2011-11-23
**Colorsettings in Unity (tip)**

OK, here is a tip on changing theme colors (workaround as long as there is no settings manager). In my opinion, and I believe many others, the Ubuntu-orange theme color for selected items etc. is not the most "sexy" color. The settings manager however does not exist any more at this point, but we can edit the three style sheets, in which the color settings are defined, directly to change the color settings.
I created a Unity launcher to get easy access to these files:

**1. **Download the attached colorsettings file, unzip it[B]
2. [/B]Copy the file to the directory      /home/*yourname*/.local/share/applications (create it if necessary)
**3. **Drag it onto the Unity launcher

Now you can access thestyle sheets easily by right-clicking on the icon.
In the style sheets (all three), look for this color:**#f07746** (that is the ugly orange :)), and replace it, for example with #646464 (anthracite) or any color you like. Use gcolor if you like, to pick and preview another color.

**warning!! do not mess around with other settings without knowing what you are doing, or you will need sunglasses **[B]\\:D/

[/B]edit: by accident I linked to a non-default icon in the .desktop file, uploaded an improved version

### Post by HDave on 2011-11-24
I have a long list of Unity problems which I will get to in a subsequent (and more constructive) post, but first a word on all this Linux "Mint" business.

Unity has pissed many people off, and so many folks are looking into alternatives such as Mint.  Thus the popularity on Distrowatch.  I looked into it as well and decided "no way!"

Why?  Because Gnome2 is gone...dead.  I am sorry for this, but this fact cannot be changed and it is not Ubuntu's fault.  Linux Mint is trying to use the MATE project which is a Gnome2 fork.  As a developer, I can tell you this is insane.  There are already a TON of issues being reported on this...[it has been delaying Mint 12 ]("http://blog.linuxmint.com/")and its only going to get worse. This time next year, even more apps built for Gnome3 are going to have compatibility problems.  Unless Mint has a spare 300+ developers, this is not an answer.

So what is an answer?

1) Gnome3 Shell -- This is an option on Mint...but it is already an option on Ubuntu.  I would LOVE to use it but I [can't]("https://bugs.launchpad.net/ubuntu/+source/nvidia-graphics-drivers/+bug/728745").  This is an nvidia issue that also breaks Gnome3 Shell on Fedora, Redhat, and Arch to name a few.  So again, Ubuntu is already doing what it can here....sit and wait for nVidia.

2) KDE -- Again, Ubuntu already offers a first class KDE desktop.

3) Unity -- The ugly truth is that, without Unity, I and many other Linux/Gnome users actually don't have a working desktop anymore!!  So here I am.  I either go back to Ubuntu 10.04 or I do what I can to make Unity work for me.  Until nVidia makes GnomeShell work with my card, I have no other choice.

You heard it here first folks, all this Linux Mint talk is a flash in the pan....now lets all get back to work and make Gnome Shell and Unity realize their awesome potential.

Edit:  Just found out that it may soon be possible to run Gnome3 Shell without 3D acceleration:
[http://news.softpedia.com/news/GNOME-Shell-No-Longer-Requires-3D-Acceleration-232838.shtml](http://news.softpedia.com/news/GNOME-Shell-No-Longer-Requires-3D-Acceleration-232838.shtml)

---

### Post by moeneim on 2011-11-24
I am using both Unity & Gnome 3 on 11.10 and I really like both. I Have been mostly using Unity lately because I like my windows to behave wobbly when touched. If I am not using Unity, I am using Gnome 3 (sadly without wobbly windows). One thing for sure, I am not going back to Gnome 2. 

Now for aesthetics, just add CCSM and a better icon theme (like AoWoken 2.3) and you end up with a beautiful ubuntu Unity desktop.

Unity will improve with every development cycle, there is a big improvement in 11.10 over 11.04.

I tried Mint 12, and with all due respect for the mint design team, I do not like the hybrid UI they came up with. I personally think it is ugly. I have never said that about any of the mint releases before 12.

### Post by craig10x on 2011-11-24
I think this last section from a recent ubuntu 11.10 with unity review i read, sums it up nicely...but first my comments and feedback:

I use to love linux mint, and it was very polished and nice looking, and agree with the previous comment that mint 12 looks kind of pasted together and not very attractive, where as my ubuntu 11.10 with unity looks great, is very polished and in fact (like this article says) i find the unity dock fun to use and inviting as it were...(lol)

Here's the conclusion section in the article:

The next Ubuntu release is an LTS (Long-Term Support) release and if it's anywhere near as good as 10.10, most Ubuntu users should wait for the release. However, I actually like this 11.10 release and I'm stoked about installing it on my netbook. Some have remarked boot up times are slower, but as a regular user of openSUSE 11.4 and Mandriva 2011, Ubuntu 11.10 still rules when it comes to startup times on a variety of machines (which makes it appropriate for my low-powered Asus EEEPC 1000H).

When it comes to hardware and performance, Ubuntu deserves its reputation as one of the most popular Linux distributions. With the switch to Unity, there is a certain loss of cutomization for the casual user - but isn't a big deal for the average Linux user. Whereas KDE is a functional and logical desktop and Gnome 3 provides a frugal and practical experience, Unity is fun and welcoming.  

Users can easily get lost in the hype and criticisms of the Unity desktop because it's the first thing Ubuntu users see. But it's important to remember that Ubuntu is more than just the desktop interface that is Unity - and taking that into consideration users would realize Ubuntu 11.10 is a keeper.

It is helpful, but it is also indicative of why some people find Unity problematic.  Why on earth should people need a cheat sheet to use a desktop which is supposed to be designed withe ease of use and new users in mind? No one would need a cheat sheet to use Windows or MacOSX.

---

### Post by snowpine on 2011-11-25
> **beew said:**
> It is helpful, but it is also indicative of why some people find Unity problematic.  Why on earth should people need a cheat sheet to use a desktop which is supposed to be designed withe ease of use and new users in mind? No one would need a cheat sheet to use Windows or MacOSX.

I would disagree with your last point... Nobody is born knowing how to use Windows or Mac, we must learn. If you are born with a type of brain similar to the brains that designed Windows, then Windows will be easier for you to learn; and so on for Mac, Unity, Gnome, KDE, etc. :)

How can keyboard shortcuts possibly be so intuitive that you can guess them without a "cheat sheet?" For example Alt+Tab switches applications, but this is not something we are instinctively born knowing. There is nothing about the shapes or labels on the Alt and Tab keys to suggest that pressing them together will switch apps, nevertheless this is a useful keyboard combination that took me a few seconds to learn and that I now use many times a day. Furthermore I can customize the keyboard shortcuts if an alternate combination of keystrokes would be more intuitive for my needs.

Until the day there is a separate key on the keyboard for each task, we will always need "cheat sheets" to learn the shortcuts.

---

### Post by beew on 2011-11-26
> **snowpine said:**

How can keyboard shortcuts possibly be so intuitive that you can guess them without a "cheat sheet?" For example Alt+Tab switches applications, but this is not something we are instinctively born knowing. There is nothing about the shapes or labels on the Alt and Tab keys to suggest that pressing them together will switch apps, nevertheless this is a useful keyboard combination that took me a few seconds to learn and that I now use many times a day. Furthermore I can customize the keyboard shortcuts if an alternate combination of keystrokes would be more intuitive for my needs.

Until the day there is a separate key on the keyboard for each task, we will always need "cheat sheets" to learn the shortcuts.

Maybe being so keyborad centric is the problem. I don't buy that people find Windows easier simply because they are more used to it, I could have made the same argument with gnome2 instead of Windows or Mac. Point and click (and touch, though not suitable for desktop for the simple reason of having to stretch your arm) is* intrinsically* easy and intuitive. I am skeptical that the "desktop metaphore" simply came into being through some arbitrary "design" fad,  I think it is more likely that it becomes widely adopted and entrenched because it meshes well with the way people actually work.

A problem many people have with Unity is that you can't do stuffs as effectively and quickly using only point and click as in the other OSes and the old gnome2 interface. In order to be as efficient you have to use keyboard shortcuts, remember random key combos and know where the keys are so that you don't need to think,-- if you think this is  a trivial point try to use a foreign keyboard,-- and that is not user friendly, as evidented by the need of a cheat sheet. 

I use Unity, but I think that is a legit point.

First and foremost beew, if you have evaluated Unity from a mouse-user's perspective and found it lacking, I think that is valuable feedback and I respect it. 

I am a keyboard guy (Ratpoison is my favorite DE ;)) so I find the keyboard-centric nature of Unity to be quite "user friendly." (I use quotes because I think the term is meaningless; every user is different!) "Press the Super key and type what you want to do" is brilliant in my opinion.

The "desktop metaphor" may have been intrinsically easy and intuitive for an office worker in Palo Alto in the 1970s/80s but it is showing its age. Many young people in 2011 don't even work at a desk! Wherever they happen to be is their "office." Increasingly we use mobile electronic devices that do not have mice (cell phones, ipods, tablets, etc.) for tasks previously done sitting at a desk in front of the computer. Furthermore the prevalence of repetitive stress injuries demonstrates the need for a variety of ergonomic interfaces catering to the needs of different types of user.

### Post by Jacobonbuntu on 2011-11-26
I do believe that an OS should have both qualities: a good intuitive (graphical) interface and a number of well-designed key combinations. People who do not spend hours per day with their computer are served by the first, people who have the opportunity to build op routine by the second. I agree with snowpine that the perception of what feels "logical" is a matter of personal experience and the age in which we are living. Like music, the notes only make 10% of the music, the other part has to be filled in by the existing common language of the musician and the listener.

As It comes to Unity, I do believe it is potentially extremely user friendly in our time, and can be handled well without a lot of "key-work". The layout is straightforward and actually a lot more logical than the old style gnome in my perception. I really do not see why any other OS would be more convenient. The point is that it is not showing its potential after a fresh install and needs to be arranged by the user. The ease of doing that is where there is a lot to gain in future versions.

---

### Post by al108 on 2011-11-30
Well, I was looking for a place where I can express all my rage about Unity, but I found a nice thread to read instead :)

There are a few things I've noticed people concerned with here - polls and mint - I agree that when people find themselves not liking Unity they will start searching elsewhere. When Unity 1st came out I looked at Mint and even tried it but reverting to Gnome was easy. With 11.10 Unity much improved but as much as I was trying to like it I couldn't handle it for very long. Maybe in the spring I'll give it a try again or maybe I'll just stay with KDE :)

If Unity supposed to be good for all kind of devices it should still be good for a desktop too. I, and all of my friends have a very strong opinion about usefulness of Unity on a desktop or a netbook. Unity is not very intuitive. Somebody mentioned here that we have to learn new interface and it is true but I and my friends didn't have to spend much time figuring out iOS or Android OS or Gnome2 or KDE for that matter. 

To those who think people don't use iOS and Android devices seriously: I used iPad as a live sound engineer to set up monitors and control the sound at gigs with anywhere between 200 and 2000 people present. I and many pros use it (as it's officially supported by Gettys Images) to get releases for photo and video production. I use my android tablet now almost exclusively for my emails and light office work. While I understand this is not a discussion of iOS or Android, I think Ubuntu community should not disregard those as toys only. :)
 
"Light" vs "Not light" DE. If I have a powerful computer do I really want to use "light" anything? I want it to be slick and show a lot of different info and be very customizable and so on. I don't necessary want it light. I have a big computer and I want to use it's capabilities. Where I would want to be light is tablets and maybe netbooks (although I always preferred installing full gnome on netbooks vs lighter DEs). ... Oh wait, my tablets have iOS and Android installed on them and I can barely tolerate those.:) I can not start to imagine how much I would hate Unity on my tablet at least not in the present form.

I'm sorry for a lot of negativity towards Unity and a long post. I would rather see Unity being an option for everybody to install just like Kubuntu, Xubuntu  and the such. I'm sure it'll get better with time. Why stick  people with it until it's ready for prime time. Which in my opinion it's pretty far from ready. I know a lot of people like it a lot so let it be as a choice not a default choice at least until it matures.

And the shortcuts, really? How they supposed to work with a touch screen? ... I think I'm going in circles :)  I hope Canonical will figure it out and I hope they won't come up with some ugly (sorry, light) replacement for KDE lol

### Post by cariboo on 2011-11-30
@al108, Unity wasn't originally designed to run on tablets, or smartphones, that's why it is so keyboard concentric now, it will take some fairly large changes to make it usable on systems with a touch screen as well as on televisions, and personally I'm not really sure how well it will work. We'll just have to wait until the 14.04 testing session starts to see what kind of progress has been made.

As a long time Ubuntu user, you should be well aware, that you aren't stuck with the default interface, it takes a couple of minutes if you are on high speed, to download one of the several desktop meta-packages that are available in the repositories.

Right on! I don't care unity or not, but I think there's lack of vision there. Remember some time ago window controls moved to the left.... Where did that take us? It didn't bother me much; but we were told it's for a good and big but unknown reason that they couldn't tell us really why. To me it just stirred things up and made many people unhappy. Same thing is happening with Unity. I'm not necessary against it, but why? We all know that today it's not a mature DE; so why make a bunch of people unhappy and for what reason? I know gnome 2 is gone and switch needed to be made but it could've been something a little more mature. 

I don't know if it's too late to make that decision for 12.04 but I think it would make a lot more sense (also a lot more business sense) to have an LTS release with a good mature DE (like XFCE). They can keep the Unity for later if they think it's so wonderful just not as a default choice for now.

---

### Post by cariboo on 2011-12-01
As to why Unity, this Wikipedia [article]("http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unity_(user_interface)#Development") comes as close as anything else as to the reasoning behind the decision.

### Post by Jacobonbuntu on 2011-12-01
....I use both Xubuntu (netbook) and Unity (on desktops). although I like Xubuntu for what it is, I do not understand the suggestion to "return" to gnome 2 -style layout. The logic of Unity is easy to explain. 

On modern (wide) screens, there is a lot of space next to the (mostly vertically orientated) documents, enough space for a vertical launcher. Why on the left? scroll bars are on the right, so don't let the launcher conflict with it.
The layout is sober, easy and logical: applications/locations on the left, small tools and indicators in the top panel on the right, application menus on the top left. Why a global menu? That way, the menu is always located at the same place.

To me it all makes complete sence. I must confess after getting used to the concept I find old style layout disturbing. 

what surprises me is the resistance of, in many cases, seemingly experienced Ubuntu/Linux users. I would expect them to be able to easily overcome the last step Ubuntu has to (and will) make to become a fully grown and fantastic Desktop environment: the fact that in this stage arranging/tweaking the system in many cases needs editing system files manually.

---

### Post by cariboo on 2011-12-01
The big problem here is that we only have 60,000 active users, so we are a small part of the whole Ubuntu community, the users that are happy with Unity aren't bothering to post how happy they are with it, and the users that dislike Unity are hoping if they post long and loud enough someone will pay attention to them.

The only real way to affect change, is to get involved in the community. You don't have to be a developer, you just have to be able to prove your points on the many mailing lists, or IRC channels that are available to us.

The Design team has recently opened up another way to contact them, see this [blog post]("http://design.canonical.com/2011/11/getting-in-touch-with-us/")

Here is a [list]("https://wiki.ubuntu.com/IRC/ChannelList") of the available IRC channels.

This is a list of [mailing lists]("https://lists.ubuntu.com/") you can subscribe to.

### Post by Scott Baker on 2011-12-02
Howdy all. I just wanted to take a moment to chime in on my feelings towards Unity. First, I don't completely dislike it. It's quite attractive actually. It normally works the way it should. But it's not the current "full time" installation for me. I use Ubuntu 11.04 for a couple of reasons. First, it does offer the option of booting into Unity. For us "old farts" set in our old ways, it gives us (me) a chance to learn to use Unity. I've been an "Ubuntoid" since the days of Fiesty, and as such, am "comfortable" with the classic desktop. I have performed a lot of 'buntu installations for others, staying with the same classic desktop for those installations. It's based on ease of use for the less computer savvy. For me, the reasons for not using Unity full time yet are pretty minor. First, it's slower than the classic. Slower to boot, slower to respond. Next, for me anyhow, it's still a bit confusing to use. Lastly, and this is the big one for me, it's not as easy to "modify". What used to be a few keystrokes at a terminal window, has now turned into a major, hit or miss undertaking. To wrap up this up, I don't HATE Unity, but it's still a bit rough. 12.04 may remove the rough edges, and by the time it's released, I should be ready for the full transition. And so everyone knows, I have tried 11.10, but after a month bumped back to 11.04 for the above reasons. #-o

---

### Post by gleedadswell on 2011-12-10
> **cariboo907 said:**
>  and to open a second instance its a right click, and select new terminal.


Or even faster is to middle mouse click to open a second instance.  One my early frustrations with Unity was that the "collapse" of window switching and opening new applications into the launcher.  Now that I've figured out a number of things (some good hotkeys and so on) I'm enjoying it.  But I didn't find it very intuitive.

---

### Post by gleedadswell on 2011-12-10
> **JRV said:**
> I would like to figure out what kind of user likes/dislikes Unity.

I know Unity was developed from the netbook remix, but I find it works better on a large monitor, I find it less useful on my netbook. Do you agree?

It seems to me that a person who keeps a lot of programs open might be less likely to like Unity. Do you think this is true?

Those are interesting questions, so here are my (hopefully interesting) answers.

I'm a pretty serious user but not a linux guru.  I do a fair bit of coding, don't mind working from the command line, can muddle my way through writing simple linux scripts and so on.  But much of the time I'm using standard applications (Office, browser, graphics programs, scientific plotting programs).

I really like Unity a lot.  I decided to give it a serious try once I saw that Gnome 2 was no more.  I'd say I hated it for the first couple of days, disliked it for another few, was ambivalent for while.  It probably took me two weeks to really start to like it.  Now I like it better than Gnome.

I keep *a lot* of programs open all at once because I often prepare very complicated documents that involve bringing together graphics, equations, results of calculations, and so on.  It is not uncommon for me to have two or three windows open on each of my four desktops, and I'm often switching between them rapidly.  At first I found this very frustrating in Unity.  That changed when I got to know the hotkeys.  In particular I think the Super+number design is brilliant.  It took me a while to discover ALT+`.  Between those two hotkeys I can switch between windows much faster and more reliably than I could in Gnome where I'd frequently find myself searching among my desktops to find the one I was looking for.

---

### Post by imaca on 2011-12-19
What I dislike:
1. Having to search for applications. I don't actually want to memorize the name of every app on my PC. I prefer a logical menu divided into categories making it easy to find everything (you know, like gnome 2).
2. Not being able access all open application windows. I've been using Cairo-dock for so long I can't remember how Gnome 2 worked, but in Cairo-dock running apps are highlighted and hovering over the app shows all sub-windows. Using unity it feels like windows are lost - I have to resort to alt-tab to find hidden dialogue boxes etc. This alone makes Unity a non starter for any beginner, who will not know the keyboard shortcut.
3. Having it stuck on the left hand side. Doesn't work for me - never will. I have used bottom located windows managers for over 20 years.
4. Menu and window buttons on the top task bar. Why would I want to go all the way to the top of my screen if I have an un-maximized window? I almost never use maximised windows on a 24" screen. Making users push their mouse further is NOT a user interface improvement.
5. Soooo Sloooow. Have just updated my old athlon XP from 10.04, the drop in performance is *Very* noticable.

---

### Post by spielball on 2011-12-19
How Unity spoiled my experience as a Linux newbie:

1. Unity slows down basic tasks.
In Unity, switching between applications is a pain in the **** when you prefer working with your mouse. Without a task bar and being in a maximized window, one easily loses overview over their open tasks and windows. Super + W is nice when you love working with the keyboard. But it sucks when you love working with the mouse. Unity makes me feel lost on the desktop. And starting an application that I don't use that often, or looking for an app that I need for a specific task (but not knowing if it's installed or not), is just plain dumb in Unity. Why does Unity force me to type into a search box? I can't remember any name of any installed app.

And if I want to see what's installed, I just want to point and click and see an overview. I don't want to be forced to open the Dash, click on the apps category, look in 'Installed Apps', click 'See More', scroll down, etc. It's a pain.

The global menu is basically a good thing. But the way it's implemented, reverses the efficiency into a time-consuming nuisance. I want to 'aim and click', not 'hover, aim and click'.

2. Unity is slow and unstable.
Unity crashes about 5 times per day on my machine. And in about 2-3 cases I have to reboot. This reminds me very much of Windows Explorer back in the 90s.

3. Unity is barely customizable.
I don't want a pre-configured Dash that leaves me no choice. I want to  be able to define what is being shown when I hit the Dash button. I'd  prefer a set of self-defined app icons (shortcuts) instead of a panel  with monster icons that are just wasting space. Also, the launcher is  like an unmodifiable brick. When you right-click it, nothing happens.  But that's what would be most intuitive as from the user's perspective.  I'd expect a context menu that offers me to customize it. Instead, I  need CompizConfig and MyUnity to do this. Can it get any more  complicated? The config settings should be part of Unity itself.

My conclusion:
Ubuntu became an embarrassment for the Linux world. I had a nice time with 10.04. But 11.10 is unbearable for daily work.

---

> How Unity spoiled my experience as a Linux newbie:

1. Unity slows down basic tasks.
In Unity, switching between applications is a pain in the **** when you prefer working with your mouse. Without a task bar and being in a maximized window, one easily loses overview over their open tasks and windows. Super + W is nice when you love working with the keyboard. But it sucks when you love working with the mouse. Unity makes me feel lost on the desktop. And starting an application that I don't use that often, or looking for an app that I need for a specific task (but not knowing if it's installed or not), is just plain dumb in Unity. Why does Unity force me to type into a search box? I can't remember any name of any installed app.

And if I want to see what's installed, I just want to point and click and see an overview. I don't want to be forced to open the Dash, click on the apps category, look in 'Installed Apps', click 'See More', scroll down, etc. It's a pain.

The global menu is basically a good thing. But the way it's implemented, reverses the efficiency into a time-consuming nuisance. I want to 'aim and click', not 'hover, aim and click'.

2. Unity is slow and unstable.
Unity crashes about 5 times per day on my machine. And in about 2-3 cases I have to reboot. This reminds me very much of Windows Explorer back in the 90s.

3. Unity is barely customizable.
I don't want a pre-configured Dash that leaves me no choice. I want to  be able to define what is being shown when I hit the Dash button. I'd  prefer a set of self-defined app icons (shortcuts) instead of a panel  with monster icons that are just wasting space. Also, the launcher is  like an unmodifiable brick. When you right-click it, nothing happens.  But that's what would be most intuitive as from the user's perspective.  I'd expect a context menu that offers me to customize it. Instead, I  need CompizConfig and MyUnity to do this. Can it get any more  complicated? The config settings should be part of Unity itself.

My conclusion:
Ubuntu became an embarrassment for the Linux world. I had a nice time with 10.04. But 11.10 is unbearable for daily work.

I'd rather not get involved in a pro / contra discussion, I will not get in detail, but most of your problems can be solved by changing settins with, yes, Compiz settings manager or MyUnity. I believe in open source it is *always *the case that the desktop is completed, if you want to see it that way, by third parties (and even also with Windows and Mac). I don't see the reason in asking Ubuntu to repeat what is done already very well by others.

---

### Post by HDave on 2011-12-22
After several weeks of playing with 11.10 I think I have come to reach a few conclusions about Unity.

1) For all the problems with launcher, it is a decent (not great) launcher.  I was very happy with AWN before, but the Unity Launcher I can live with as long as it steadily improves.

2) The common window bar and menus at the top i just can't get used to, but a couple of commands and poof...thats all gone.  So I am really don't have any issues there other than the lack of a nice configuration and settings tool.

3) The only aspect of Unity that is still grating on my nerves is Dash.  This is a very, very poor excuse for a finder/launcher and I am appalled every time I open it.  It is very slow, provides no indication when it is done searching, and takes 3 clicks and several keystrokes to start an app.  If I spend a few moments to get it to look like I want, the next time I click on it its back to its original generic self.  Its like I know what I want to do and Dash keeps fighting me....trying to always get in the way.  It's like it came from the Federal Government's Department of Launchers.

For me, 90% of the pain of Unity can be found in Dash.   From now on I'm not going to deride Unity anymore...I'm going to pick on Dash.

p.s.

I know there are lots of keyboard shortcuts to do certain things and I see in the 12.04 Alpha they display a help screen to assist with that!!  How ridiculous!  Does anyone need a help screen to use 10.04?  Does anyone need a help screen for their Android tablet?

---

### Post by HDave on 2011-12-22
For me, its not leaving mainstream linux that is the problem, but leaving the desktop that is the problem.

Granted, most of this pain is a result of Gnome3 and not Ubuntu, but it was Ubuntu's choice to target Unity at tablets and instead of desktops.  This was/is a huge mistake as Android and iOS already own that market.  Already I find the quality and features of Android apps to be as good as, or in some cases, better than the web or desktop versions. Yet Canonical is positioning themselves so as to make Google their #1 competitor.

Really, I can't believe that Canonical put all this effort into tablets when Android is already there.  IMO that effort could have been spent on realizing Canonical's (now totally forgotten) dream of an Android execution environment.  That project would have provided a synthesized Ubuntu/Android environment where we get the best of both worlds, but on a traditional large screen, multi-processor CPU, keyboard & mouse driven computer.

Sorry for straying off-topic...

### Post by cmcanulty on 2011-12-22
It seems to me most of the pain was caused by releasing unity and gnome 3 way too early. I have been working long hours just to get Ubuntu 11.10 into a form that our patrons won't freak out about. I seem to have finally tweaked the backlight problem. In the past I was eager for the new release and all the new usability features. Now I live in dread of releases. I plan to go to 12.04 classic as soon as it is stable and hopefully stick with it 5 years until everything improves. The amount of work needed to go from the 11.10 release to a usable stable system is a lot and I think many casual users would give up and go to windows or downgrade.My big wish is that either gnome 3 or canonical will decide to release a new stable desktop edition for power users and have it be a permanent option. Otherwise we get cabled together tweaks that will get more and more unstable as the mainstream editions pull in a different direction. If someone had told me how negative and disappointed I would be about Linux and Ubuntu this year I never would have believed them.

### Post by kazuya on 2011-12-23
I know this is supposed to be constructive criticism and not ranting, but I am super dissappointed in the state of linux overall. At least from the newbie to intermediate level. I feel a major step back from the great advances and improvement made rom 2004 to 2010. 2011 was a huge step back in my opinion for a number of reasons on the gnu/linux part.

(1) Unity and Gnome 3 - great in terms of attempting to do something radical, innovative, and a focus on consistency with look. Sadly, it took away the ease of customization for users. This part is not so bad anymore for me due the numerous extensions. - I use Pinguy OS now and then as a speedy work around to bypass this lack of customizationability.

(2) Very unforgiving is the seemingly overly bloatedness of the interface. Unity at least performs a little faster than gnome 3. I personally prefer the look of gnome 3 to unity. - This bloatedness is across the board with most of the cutting edge distros using 3.0 kernel and above. The 2.6.36 kernel where much better than the newer kernels for power management and performance.

(3) PCs and laptops with ATI/AMD drivers are almost hopeless to install on. To Unity's credit, the ATI drivers face bigger issues with gnome 3. Across the board a better job could have been done by AMD as well as linux
distro developers to ensure functionality before release. In the past, users at least had a safe graphics mode of install that worked. Today, that is not the case. On the llano-based AMD laptops, using the nomodeset in boot option was the only way to successfully install Ubuntu / Mint / Pinguy OS. Archbang installed on these llano machines without much issues except for the sound.

(4) Insane CPU spikes when running flash videos from web browsers is a major let down. This is the case on almost all distros I have tried.

In summary, linux just feels bloated and slow now this year than ever before. I am hoping this changes soon. I have used other desktop environments now such as e17, lxde, xfce4, and my favorite for a while, openbox. Kde4, Gnome 3, and Unity are simply too bloated for my machines.

Nvidia, ATI and adobe(flash) are big culprits to the discomfort I and I'm sure many users have with linux today. We need some thorough testing on older and newer machines before release of kernels, DEs, and/or distros.

I agree with previous post, gnome 3 and unity were in fact released way too fast without sufficient transition time. And they both make me run my laptop like a 32 bit non multi-tasking device. Everything feels slower and takes longer to get to my applications. 

I recommend a lighter, customizable, but more importantly stable Desktop environment.

In my case right out of the box the first thing I wanted to do was play a game that I like and noticed that the game screen was just slightly under the top bar window which you cannot hide on unity

So in order to make games and other applications that need that top space to expand to full screen you need to install compiz-manager-settings from the software center

Then select Workarounds, Legacy, Full Window

This fixes it and is simple to install for those who are aware of it and who can again hunt down the info.

Although many other things that I use the computer for work well, 2 of the main things for me is trading and a game which worked perfectly on all previous ubuntu but not on ubuntu 11.10

I'm not complaining I'm learning to live with it and starting to enjoy how it works, but it still needs some work

I'll test with some other games but it seems like the dual core processors are getting ate up jumping around in the 50% to 90% range on one processor and the other is always running in the 20% even before I play a game so thats not great

Again not complaining it's likely I need a new computer to keep up but really didn't want to do that right now, but I will if I have to.

Happy blogging

### Post by kazuya on 2011-12-24
I agree with everyone. In reality, all the linux distros suffer this same issue. Windows 8 is supposedly faster than windows 7,. Even Microsoft learnt the hard way that discriminating against older hardware users would lose you customers. That is why Vista was an utter failure. I have to say Gnome 3.2, Unity, and the 3.0 kernels all contribute towards similar bloatedness as Vista. They are not quite as bad as Vista, but the total combination of bloat in the codes and designs severely ruins the performance, stability, and functionality that for years we had come to enjoy.

Salix and Debian-based distros sporting the older more proven Desktop environments, kernels, and applications suffer less from these bloat. 

The other external culprits beyond the control of the linux developers and distros are bloated adobe flashplayer, extremely poor ati/amd & nvidia videocard driver supports. The sooner these third party components get opensourced, the sooner the progress and less dependence on them. As long as gnu/linux depends on proprietary solutions, we would never be totally free of these recurrent instabilities. I hope other vendors come into the market outside of adobe, ati, and nvidia. That is when things would really get better as far as interoperability. ATI Catalyst drivers for example supposedly render better power management for windows laptops than linux counterpart. I have tested and seen this on the newer llano-based laptops.

Distros like Ubuntu need to involve their users in the future before making drastically poor decisions as evident in 11.04 and 11.10. I cannot speak for others, but they lost me almost completely with it.

I still will not forget what they had done for linux growth and push towards user friendliness. This is what makes it even more disheartening.

### Post by Jacobonbuntu on 2011-12-29
Hi people,

although I really like Unity, it has some practical tresholds for, specially, new users. One important thing is that the (default) Home icon in the launcher has only one directory (home), which makes traveling through your directories a bit of a handicapped experience. Of course you can manually edit the nautilus-home.desktop file, but that is not what you have in mind, thinking of easy configuration.

I created a script that makes the job easy, you can make a great Home launcher with a quick list to your favorite directories in no time, just by telling the script what directories you want to appear in the list. No knowledge of the .desktop file is needed. also you can add dividers, or create new directories and add them to the quick list at the same time.

although the script has no "chique" interface (yet), it works fine and fast. I plan to keep working on it.
you can download the script [[here] ]("http://bazaar.launchpad.net/%7Evlijm/jonb.homelauncher/launchermaker/files"), to use it, open terminal, cd to its directory and type the command: "python launchermaker.py"
**n.b.** you may have to give it executable rights (right-click the file, "properties")
you can just try it out, until the very end of the script you have the option to cancel.

I hope someone likes it :)

have a good time,
Jacob

### Post by rectec794613 on 2011-12-31
Here's my story. I hope it's not to long. Hear me out:

Well I'd had it of all the error messages, crashes, glitches, bugs and freezes of KDE...

I was sick of Linux's user-unfriendlyness. I was sick of having 3 different installs with 3 different DEs, with none being up-to-par. XFCE is outdated and ugly. Low compatibility and full of bugs. I only installed it so I could use Compiz. KDE, while nice looking and nearly modern, is severely lacking in user experience. The whole thing feels... cheap and unstable. Although I cannot say how much I love what the KDE team has done with KDE SC, well let's face it. It's a buggy and crashy nightmare. I get a crash with an error message *every time* I shut down. Ugh. The third DE, Gnome Shell, is near-perfect. Solid, stable, simple. But I felt like I wasn't a part of Ubuntu. The whole thing felt rather empty...

I had nearly given up. I had shunned Unity early after I tried it. But I knew Canonical's goal was to create a user-friendly OS. This I needed badly. So I burned a copy of Ubuntu 11.10 AMD64 _alternate._ (Alternate because Linux has horrible drivers for my network card that cripple my system upon bootup if I don't have NVIDIA drivers installed.) I put the disc into the drive and restarted. The install went fine, and I went into recovery mode to install GFX drivers, rebooted and was greeted with the Unity desktop.

I was ready to begin anew. I would have a completely new mindset. I had reached the peak of my power-user experience, customizing and tweaking my systems past the breaking point one too many times. I wanted to start fresh. Like back in the good ol' days with my first time using Ubuntu back in 2007 with Gutsy. I wanted to become a noob again, basically.

I simulated how a new user would act with Ubuntu. Unity is supposed to be a very user-friendly DE, after all. I thought practically, as did the UI. On the Dash if you want to browse the web, you just click "Browse the Web." Simple. I did things like I would on Windows, set up my Social accounts, read some of the help docs. And you know what? I had a great experience. Errors non-existant, simple and easy to use, solid interface. **Exactly** what a computer should be like. I can't believe it took us all these years to get here. But here we are. Unity.

Here's my ultimate point: Ubuntu is seeing a substantial rise in new users. And new users expect everything to be as easy to use as Windows or Mac OS. And for a long while, we, the Open Source community have failed them. Bewildering them with error messages and software installation, frustrating them with freezes and crashes, and then scaring them away with bugs. But I think now, with Ubuntu and Unity, we have given new users a place where they can be comfortable. Bewildering them only with the simplicity and practicality of the Dash. We keep them connected with the Me Menu. And we keep them productive with the LibreOffice suite while putting use of the workspaces that Linux has blessed us with. 

While some may argue that Canonical are making bad decisions with having Unity as the default Shell for Ubuntu, I think Ubuntu is right where it should be. New users can be comfortable with the easy to use Unity interface, while power users remain free to choose their own DE to suit there needs. Canonical: don't change a single thing.

Thank you.

---

### Post by coffeecat on 2012-01-05
> **AgentZ86 said:**
> YOU CAN't Unity makes every screen that is open cover the upper tool bar so you have to minimize everything to get to your desktop tool bar

No, it doesn't. Only maximised windows cover the upper panel. Don't minimise your windows, but unmaximise them and then resize them (if necessary) to your ideal working size. In 11.10 (but not in 11.04), if you open CCSM -> Desktop -> Ubuntu Unity Plugin -> Experimental tab, you can change the automaximise value. By default, if a window is sized to 75% or greater than the maximised value and then closed, it will re-open maximised. I find 75% to be too low as a threshold and find 80-85% to be about right. Then unmaximised windows will open unmaximised to their previous size, so long as this is below the chosen threshold.

Of course, this is on a desktop-sized monitor. If you are talking about a netbook screen, then most applications are going to be maximised anyway.

And besides:

> **AgentZ86 said:**
> Is there not simpler way to get to the tool bar or bookmarks without having to minimize everything all the time.

If you have several windows open, all maximised, simply move the mouse pointer to the left of the screen to cause the launcher to pop out, and then click on the icon for the app you want to be focussed.

---

### Post by oxman on 2012-01-07

The one reason I hated unity was that it didn't offer me options. That's a big deal to me. As I discover various work arounds (like making the icons smaller etc) I'm warming up to it. I always liked the ability to put my own icons in for various application icons on my desktop. I haven't figured out how to do that yet. ETC. Final declaration is still out. 
I have noticed a few bugs. I couldn't minimize the disk utility app after I had maxed it. The versatility of accessing the desk tops gave me some mobility to continue my work in spite of the frozen app. Ctrl+Alt+Backspace was the only option I could find to finally clear the locked up utility. I couldn't find the utility app in my processes so couldn't kill it. Dealing with it was a step up from Gnome 2 in the end.
Onward!

### Post by StewartM on 2012-01-14
> **MG&TL said:**
> 

d) Looking back, I see every other DE being criticised at a new release. Even GNOME2, from whatever GNOME1 was. So...give it time, and then there will be uproar at Unity being ditched.

Hmm. I thought made another reply to this thread, and it doesn't show. 

I don't think that the backlash to Unity can be explained away as just a case of "Baby Duck Syndrome" (i.e., users 'imprinting' on their first DE experience). Especially here, as I'd say most people's first "imprint" was with Windows and not even a Linux DE.

Nor is it because the complainers think that Unity is ugly. On the contrary, I think it and GNOME 3 are quite attractive and have "cool" features--"cool", to say, from an eye-candy perspective. But when you start counting the number of mouse clicks and/or keyboard actions you have to perform to get something done, a traditional desktop layout like that presented by KDE, GNOME 2.3, Xfce, or LXDE once configured beats what you can accomplish with Unity or GNOME 3. It just takes fewer actions to do the same things.

That is why I suggested un-auto-hiding the launcher to be a good first step. It at least means that you reduce tne number of action it takes to switch between running apps to just one click as opposed to two (ALT + TAB then select; or SUPER + W and select). Minimizing windows to a panel or laucher that's always visible for easy switching may not be great eye candy, but it's very utilitarian. I was involved in an IM chat the other day in Unity while discussing my music playing and also browsing web pages and before I un-autohid the launcher, switching between Pidgin, Rhythmbox, and Firefox using the Unity default settings just about drove me nuts. Once I made the launcher always visible, switching was simply a single click.

I suspect that what you say about Unity being missed one day may be true. However, that will probably be becuase Unity will have become a better interface by that time, not because users just at long last saw the light. It is very true that yes, GNOME 2 was criticized when it first came out, and there was a firestorm about KDE 4's release. But GNOME 2.3 was a nice polished interface compared to earlier predecesors and KDE 4.7 has incorporated improvements since its rocky start. Right now the biggest advantage of Unity I see is that it has the useful indicator applet (GNOME 3 will get that in 12.04)  and that it's well-integrated with GNOME apps (GNOME-fallback, surprisingly, isn't).  

But for improvement to come, the Ubuntu developers have to realize that the smartphone/tablet touchscreen interface is *NOT* superior to the traditional desktop layout. Far from it, it is inhernetly *clunkier*, more prone to error, and more difficult to use. It may look cool, but it takes more actions to get the same job done. Mark Shuttleworth may be one of those who believe in the "death of the desktop" as his blog recently has inferred, and that in the future we'll all be happily using tablets and smartphones to access the internet, but such beliefs I think are based on a fundamental mis-interpretation fo the data. 

StewartM

---

### Post by StewartM on 2012-01-14
> **mcduck said:**
> Actually you can still create Launchers usign graphical tool, and the tools used are exactly the same as before (the oly difference is that you don't get the option for new launcher in right-click menu, most likely because Gnome 3 doesn't use desktop icons (including launchers) by default.

Still, Alacarte works just like before, and if you want to create a launcher from right-click menu you can create an empty one in your Templates directory. Taht allows you to right-click, select Templates->new launcher, and then just right-click the newly created launcher to access it properties and configure the icon, command etc. there.

Thank you for the info. But do you mean a whole new launcher dock? Or just another app in the left-hand launcher dock? I can do the latter  easily already; in fact, already did.

The reason why I'm a bit confused is that Mark Shuttleworth was quoted as shooting down any consideration of allowing launcher docks to be placed on the right, top, or bottom of the screen. No where else but the left (thought there is already a hack that supports a bottom launcher dock).

>  But I can sure see how annoying it would be if one tried to use it with the mouse. I suppose the idea behind the design is that power users would quickly learn the few simple keyboard shortcuts it takes (and would most likely prefer shortcuts over mouse navigation anyway), while the mouse navigation and interface is designed more towards less technically oriented people.

The problem is with that (and it this applies even more to GNOME 3 than Unity, where a lot of key commands can only be executed via shortcut) is that menus are a lot easier to find, navigate, and understand for first-time users (and with Unity, most of us fall into that category). Keyboard shortcuts do have a place, but their place is to provide a shortcut for menu navigation and generally not to be the sole route to getting a task done. You shouldn't have to paste a copy of all the relevant shortcuts to the side of your box to do basic stuff.

StewartM

### Post by bryanl on 2012-01-14
re: "*The problem is with that ... is that menus are a lot easier to find, navigate, and understand for first-time users*" 

This gets to the key issue I think. -- how do you know what is "easier" for any particular group of users? How is that measured and what are the properties of that measurement?

The trend is away from a tree structured menu towards a search and context paradigm from what I can see in many UI's. Why would that be if it were more difficult? What are the presumptions in each case about the users and what they know and how they function?

As an example, compare the number of entries in the menu system in Gnome 2 distributions to the number of executables in /bin and /usr/bin -- the mismatch means there are very very many utilities and applications that are not in the menu system. How do you access them (and that gets into Gnome terminal shortcuts and methods to help with this).

It is a multi-variate issue with a big overlap in human cognition. Fascinating stuff where theories can only go so far and there is much learning to be achieved.

---

### Post by trikster_x on 2012-01-14
> **bryanl said:**
> re: "*The problem is with that ... is that menus are a lot easier to find, navigate, and understand for first-time users*" 

This gets to the key issue I think. -- how do you know what is "easier" for any particular group of users? How is that measured and what are the properties of that measurement?

A Unity type interface is "easier" for the touch-interface user group. A hover to expand type interface is easier for the mouse-interface user group. That's not an assumption, its an observation based on the difference in complexity for the user groups. Number of actions necessary should be the single defining measurement of difficulty for a user group.

> The trend is away from a tree structured menu towards a search and context paradigm from what I can see in many UI's. Why would that be if it were more difficult? What are the presumptions in each case about the users and what they know and how they function?

It's not because search and context is simpler. It's because of the proliferation of those god-forsaken tablets. Canonical is preparing to move into the tablet and mobile device market. Therefore they are creating a user experience that is simply more beneficial to a user that lacks a mouse. Keyboard shortcuts have been implemented, not because they want power users to be able to do things as fast and effortlessly as on Gnome, because the non-touch interface users will need a way to access things without having to be hindered by the fact they are using a mouse.

> As an example, compare the number of entries in the menu system in Gnome 2 distributions to the number of executables in /bin and /usr/bin -- the mismatch means there are very very many utilities and applications that are not in the menu system. How do you access them (and that gets into Gnome terminal shortcuts and methods to help with this).

These were almost always programs that were hidden for the benefit of the novice user. They are programs that are capable of changing config files or otherwise wreaking havoc on a system if used without more than a passing knowledge of the system. It was easy enough to include them in your menus by right clicking the category and adding whichever installed programs were missing from the menu. Also keep in mind there are a great number of terminal programs that have no GUI and therefore are pointless to include in the dropdown menu system.

### Post by StewartM on 2012-01-15
> **bryanl said:**
> re: "*The problem is with that ... is that menus are a lot easier to find, navigate, and understand for first-time users*" 

This gets to the key issue I think. -- how do you know what is "easier" for any particular group of users? How is that measured and what are the properties of that measurement? 

I differ with you, I think there are objective measures of how easy something is to learn and use, that it's not simply random user preference. There's the measure of "is the thing indicated by a visible marker or sign?" and the measure of "how many actions does it require to get something done"? Using both measures, Unity and GNOME 3 alike are less user-friendly than GNOME 2.3, KDE, GNOME Classic, or even LXDE.

A keyboard shortcut you have to know *a priori*. Menus you can dig through and find what you're looking for. What do you think is easier, if you don't already know and have no reference? Hunting through menus, or trying out every possible keyboard shortcut?

It's the old rejoinder to Windows advocates talking about how Windows is  more "intuitive" than the competition. But what's intuitive about CTRL + ALT + DeL? Nothing at all. 

When I started using Linux, back in 2007, it was with GNOME 2.18, which was far less polished and less user-friendly than 2.3.  But as an utter newbie I could figure out "hey, when I click on this in the menu, that happens" or "hey, I can switch between workspaces when I click on those little doo-dads down there on the bottom panel". I made mistakes, to be sure, but no unrecoverable ones.

But with GNOME 3? Even though now I'm far more experienced now, it's a struggle because to do simple tasks you must already *know* the shortcut and in effect have to know the answer. In fact, they have published a cheat sheet for it.

[http://live.gnome.org/GnomeShell/CheatSheet](http://live.gnome.org/GnomeShell/CheatSheet)

I don't think using a truly "intuitive" UI requires refering to a cheet sheet taped to your box for basic commands. Yes, there are things we all have to just learn with any new experience, but basic stuff should be staring back at you from the screen. That's the rule we apply everywhere else in life--if it's visible and obvious, then it's easy, if it's hidden, it's not.

But as Trikster_x rightly said, Unity and GNOME 3 aren't really designed for desktops/laptops or even netbooks. They are designed for tablets and smartphones, based on the mistaken belief that "the desktop is dead" and that everyone will move to using mobile devices. They are also designed with the mistaken belief that the UIs for these devices are superior to traditional DEs when in fact it's inherently clunkier. Mark Shuttleworth has inferred this kind of thinking in his blog:

-----

"A constantly changing world

The way we access the Internet, connect to our friends, listen to music, watch films and go about our daily lives is rapidly evolving. We now use a diverse set of devices with an array of operating systems, which have a range of connectivity. Few people are exclusively loyal to a single technology provider.

Consider this quote from Paul Maritz of VMWare:

    &#8220;Three years ago over 95 percent of the devices connected to the Internet were personal computers. Three years from now that number will probably be less than 20 percent. More than 80 percent of the devices connected to the Internet will not be Windows-based personal computers.&#8221; Paul Maritz, 29 August 2011 VM World Keynote.

Make no mistake &#8211; just as the world is changing for manufacturers so is it changing for Linux distributions. Today, 70% of people in Egypt access the Internet solely via the phone. Even in the US that figure is a startling 25%."

-----

These factoids may be true taken on face value. But they are misleading. 

For starters, country-by-country preference about how one connects to the internet is determined by a number of factors--the relative price of the device and the relative price of connectivity. I don't know about Egypt, but in China mobile devices are expensive, but mobile connectivity is cheap. So if you can scape up the money to buy a smartphone, that's a cost-effective way to go. In many places of the world, you access the internet at a cybercafe or other wifi spot, so having a mobile device is almost essential.

In the US, by contrast, home connectivity is far cheaper than mobile connectivity and desktops are cheaper and more powerful than smartphones or tablets. Even netbooks are cheaper than tablets or smartphones! Based on those facts alone, and I don't see the paradigm shift that Shuttleworth and others predict. This is especially true if the price of home connectivity in the developing world drops and people can access the internet at home instead of at the cybercafe.

Secondly, looking at the number of internet-connected devices sold is a misleading indicator of preference. It is more a measure of the useful lifespan of the device. A desktop computer provides more bang for the buck on inital purchase, and it can be upgraded and repaired by the user (or cheaply by a service). It can be useful for up to a decade. Mobile devices are the opposite--more likely to be broken, more fragile, harder and costly or even impossible to repair, limited or no upgradeability. A hypothetical user who sought to keep one smartphone, one tablet, one laptop, and one desktop might need over the course of 10 years need buy only 1 desktop, but might need to buy 2 laptops, 3 tablets, and 4 smartphones. But that buying habit should not be interpreted to mean that he/she prefers smartphones 4 times more than he prefers desktops. Nor should it be interpreted to mean that that he/she thinks the UI of the smartphone or tablet is superior to that of the desktop. 

Nor is this "hypothetical", because I know young people in college who have these items. And no one I know thinks that they don't need desktops. In fact, one who has a smartphone, a netbook, and a laptop called me from grad school Tuesday about buying a new desktop. He says the desktop he needs to do a whole host of things from writing papers to webpage maintenance that none of the other devices can easily manage. We talked about this, and if he had to drop anything, it would be his laptop. The netbook he uses to take notes in class and the smartphone is mostly just used as a phone. Nor do he and his peers see the smartphone/tablet UI as superior to the traditional desktop UI. They realize that it's more limited in fact, due to limitations imposed by lack of screen space.

So in my perspective, the whole raison d'etre of the Unity and GNOME 3 interface is based on a mistaken interpretation of the data. 

StewartM

### Post by hzFVOW00pw on 2012-01-17
The whole Unity/GS thing has turned out rather well for me personally.

I did not like Unity/GS for the following reasons:

- Not easily customizable, you have to do too much tweaking
- Too much clicking to find an application
- Too much mousetravel
- Those zooming windows on a big screen make me nervous
- Text based application search is nothing better than a terminal with tab-completion
- Unity global menus and overlay scrollbars are clumsy
- Stiff hardware requirements

Of course I could have just switched to Xubuntu, but there were other things about Ubuntu that I didn't like:

- Too many bugs in each fresh release, and long standing bugs that did not get fixed
- Feature creep like Ubuntu One, music stores, software stores, etc.
- I am not a fan of the half yearly release cycle, I really don't want to upgrade/install every half year.
- The Lucid LTS version is already outdated, I had accumulated a long list of PPA's to stay up to date.

The Unity/GS thing was really a trigger to look further and install a new distro with a DE suited for a *desktop* and try to stick with it for the next 10 years. I tested some distros in VirtualBox for at least a month each and Debian Testing (Wheezy) with XFCE came out as a winner. It is a rolling distro so you do not have to install every half year, and it is surprisingly stable.

It is not suited for the novice user however, I had to tweak and fix many things after install. Second, you get updates every day and probably that's too risky and/or difficult to handle for novice users. But now it is running stable with more up-to-date applications than Lucid LTS.

I will probably never look back to Ubuntu for my personal desktop use, because I have the knowhow to make Debian running smoothly. But for my n00b friends/clients I will probably still have to install Precise/XFCE (they have absolutely no need for Unity/GS) due to the (assumed) safer and more stable updates...

For a desktop, I feel there's no need for Unity/GS. But for tablets there is a need for a *free* OS/distro, because Android is really giving me the creeps, with its half open model and its integration with the Google services. A month ago I bought a cheap tablet and I am eagerly wating for Ubuntu to release a tablet distro that would make me independent from Android/Google services...

---

### Post by Handssolow on 2012-01-18
I'm seeing lots of posts by established Ubuntu users not liking Unity and looking to leave Ubuntu without any mention that they're tried the Gnome-session-fallback package. Expressions of dislike of Unity seems to end with the post or thread being shipped off to some back water of the Forum. 

I don't like Unity, it feels like going into a shop with very little on display and having to spell out for the shopkeeper the things I want instead of pointing and saying "I want to buy one of those". For the time being I can make 11.10 look like I like it.

1.Thankfully the gnome-fallback-session is available in 11.10 but not by default. In 11.04 Ubuntu Classic was available as an alternative. This time in 11.10 the package has to be downloaded as it's not by default available in Synaptic.

2.In System Tools>System Settings>Appearance, I prefer the lighter look of Radiance to the white on black of Ambiance.

3.Where's the waste bin gone? It's restored by installing the Gnome Tweak Tools package then Applications>Other>Advanced Settings>Desktop then select "Trash icon visible on desktop".

4a.Firefox and Evolution launchers on the top toolbar? Yes please. Alt+right click>Add to Panel>Custom Application Launcher. Commands for me here were firefox %u and evolution %u. The appropriate icons soon appeared.

4b. I like a Force Quit Launcher on the top toolbar. The command is now xkill and the icon is available if you left click in properties and select the Force Quit icon.

5. I don't like being automatically logged off if I leave the computer for 5 minutes etc. If I used my desktop in a public place then things would be different. System Tools>System Settings>Screen and undo the lock.

6. I did remove Thunderbird and continue with Evolution but if I were doing a fresh install I wouldn't bother. There's a bit of sorting out to do to get Evolution working properly again.

My 11.10 here looks like familiar Ubuntu and it's looking like the gnome-session-fallback package will be available in 12.04. If I used a tablet, netbook or mobile phone I'd probably prefer Unity. On a desktop I think Unity is yuc.

I'm sticking with Ubuntu on desktops as long as there's gnome-session-fallback or something similar.

---

> I'm sticking with Ubuntu on desktops as long as there's gnome-session-fallback or something similar.

Helpful post...thanks. I would add the following:

7) I use Unity 2D and wanted the launcher to always be there.  The tweak was found [here]("http://askubuntu.com/questions/62941/how-do-i-set-the-launcher-to-always-show-in-unity-2d").

8 ) I also have a big problem with the global menu.  It doesn't  make sense on my 27 inch monitor, nor on my laptop's 12.1 inch display.  The tweak is simply:  ```
sudo apt-get remove appmenu-gtk3 appmenu-gtk appmenu-qt
```

9) I missed my weather and system monitor indicators...which I later discovered were in the software center.

10) I also wanted a show desktop icon on the launcher.  Tweak [here.]("http://askubuntu.com/questions/29969/is-there-a-unity-equivalent-of-the-gnome-panel-show-desktop-applet"). 

11) Also didn't need to take up valuable panel space with my name.  Tweak [here.]("http://askubuntu.com/questions/67133/remove-user-session-indicator-in-unity")

As you can see, the fixes are out there.  That said, I have to say that I have recently switched to Xubuntu.  If you want a desktop environment that was like Gnome2, only more reliable, more feature rich, and faster...you seriously need to give Xubuntu a try.  I regret not having switched a year ago.  

I'm sure I'll be trying out 12.04 when it comes out, but in the meantime I can use Xubuntu with ZERO TWEAKS and be perfectly happy, and get back to work.

---

### Post by Myrddin Emrys on 2012-01-19
> **HDave said:**
> Yes, but in 6 months, nobody will because you can't fork a platform...it doesn't work.
 Trinity, the KDE3 fork, has been running since April 2010, and put out a release as recently as November. There's no reason why this type of project can't be kept going indefinitely if someone takes the trouble, though many will just use MATE to bridge the gap until 'sane interfaces' are fully restored to Gnome 3.
  > **HDave said:**
> Apps will evolve and be unable to run on Gnome2 apis then Mate will have to become a Gnome3/Gnome2 combo.  How many committers does MATE have?  If it less than 150, it'll never make it.
 Which apps? Nearly all packages that are not actual components of Gnome will run on anything, from twm to KDE. The major Gnome components have already been forked by MATE. It doesn't matter if a future version of Nautilus doesn't run under MATE, as long as Caja continues to work. MATE doesn't need to be a large project if it's just concerned with maintaining security and compatibility and fixing bugs in mature code (Trinity only has a handful of developers).
  > **HDave said:**
> Mint will drop Mate by this time next year and it'll end up being Ubuntu without Unity.  Meantime Unity will continue to improve.
 Mint, I think, will become 'Ubuntu with Cinnamon'. MATE isn't (and won't become) the default, but there's no reason to drop it from the respositories as long as it continues to be maintained upstream. Unity will certainly continue to improve, though it's very unlikely to be adopted by the broader Linux community.
  > **HDave said:**
> If you don't like Unity (and I don't) then go back to 10.04 or try KDE or Xubuntu.  I'm on Xubuntu and loving it right now, but am continually trying Unity and expect it will improve with time.  Mint is nonsense.
 I also like Xfce, but there's no reason not to use an up to date Ubuntu with MATE if you prefer Gnome 2. I'd be surprised if MATE does not outlast the 10.04 support cycle. Far from being 'nonsense', Mint should be applauded for making serious attempts to provide their users with the type of Gnome-based desktop 'metaphor' that many obviously prefer, whether by Gnome 3 extensions, Cinnamon or MATE.
