### Post by AgentZ86 on 2012-01-23
I tried to make Unity work

It just doesn't do what Gnome classic can do unfortunately

Top bar windows lists are not there, bottom bar windows lists are not there.

It looks nice but the logistics of the interface are just terrible.

Who wants to memorize shortcuts too ? 

The logistics of moving the mouse and browsing through menus has a reason behind it.

The logic of even webpages that do that.

Are we going to now see Ebay with some sort of memorized shortcut scheme just to figure out how to use the pages NO

It's a universal language logistics that needs NO changing.
The visual logistics of simply browsing your menu system is the key to a user friendly OS

Unity is NOT user friendly and limits the user from doing what they would do naturally which is click a mouse button, not reach up to the keyboard for shortcuts.

I was determined to make Unity work in spite of the interface but I learned some about it, I use the computer for my business to and you just can't use it with ease.
It's clunky, it's awkward and illogical for PC computings

Might be good for touch screens but not for PC's

Example:
I want to open gthumbs, ok hit the special windows key, type gthumbs, or click the icon that I put in the dash

You want to right click and move images to a folder on the network but you can't because the network is not mounted yet.

So you have go to the panel, click the folders because while the gthumbs window is open you can't access the top panel windows to browse to your places and mount the network
So you have to go to the side panel and click the folders, and then you have to open the nautalus browser finally and then select your network bookmark from you side bar
Then the and only then finally you can move your images to the networked folder

But everything is like this, all these extra step for something that you normally just click on places and select your bookmark too simply really. How much change can you want if it makes every single task harder and more time consuming.

Anyhow just ranting on, but I learned Unity and concluded that I can't stand it.
Thats my opinion, change for the worst is still change not worth changing.

Like you I tried hard to stick with Unity.  Very hard...for many months.

Having recently switched to Xubuntu, I have to say that I find the (customary) cascading hierarchy of menu items to be amazingly fast and intuitive.  It's refreshing.  People think about things in hierarchies...org charts, biological classification, governmental structures, directories, functional breakdowns.  It's an old-fashioned user interface paradigm for a reason -- IT JUST WORKS.  I can run any of 200+ programs with two clicks and no typing.  Beat that Dash!

As I've said previous in this thread, I can take almost anything about Unity except Dash.  Dash...just...doesn't...work.

### Post by leth on 2012-01-23
I just wanted to throw my two cents in here; I like Unity.  It has a handful of weird little quirks/bugs that don't make the experience as smooth as it should be (which will get worked out over time,) but I like the actual concept of the dash and the bar along the top and how they've packaged everything together.

Everyone is complaining about the lack of customization in Unity.  I can see how that would bother someone after becoming so used to the things that the old interface offered.  I'm coming back to the Linux desktop world after being in the Windows world for many years (it's been about 9 years since I ran Linux on my desktop), so I guess I'm just not as hung up on the way things were prior to Unity.

When I was trying to pick a distribution to use for coming back to a  Linux Desktop, I tried Ubuntu/Unity along with Debian-Gnome and Kubuntu  and Mint.  Ubuntu/Unity appealed to me the most.

Ubuntu/Canonical seems focused on making Linux easy to install and use and I applaud them for that.  It's finally getting to the point that I could see Ubuntu getting used on my mother's PC.  I hope Canoncial doesn't abandon Unity due to all the negative feedback.  There are other options (gnome-classic mode or whatever it's called, kubuntu, lubuntu, etc) so why not just use those options instead of expecting Unity to disappear and Ubuntu to go back to the way it was?

### Post by Hylas de Niall on 2012-01-25
I have been using 11.10 on an old netbook since the beta 2 release with only one (passing) issue to speak of.

On that device I found it to be stable, perfect for the small screen - barring certain of the save dialogs being too big - and an ideal interface. In fact, i found it to be such a great experience that i installed it on my desktop machine where i had a whole different experience:

Constant random freezes with no recourse but to repeatedly cold-boot the machine, stupidly slow start-up and shut-down times, and programs loaded slowly too. I tried reinstalls from different source iso's, different DE's, posted my issues on here, but nobody could find anything amiss with my system.

After such a good experience on my tiny netbook I was gutted.

Unity - though i got used to the layout on my 19" screen - was awkward and counter-intuitive after using panel based interfaces for so long. I would have trained myself to master it in the long run had it not been for the constant crashing. This particular installation had me convinced there was an issue with my *hardware*, despite it running Win7 with all the bells and whistles without any problems.

So, off the desktop it came, and back *on* went 10.10 which, by comparison, runs like a thoroughbred racehorse, is brilliantly responsive, and starts up and shuts down like lightening.  And *no crashes at all*.

My conclusions? 11.10 is STILL buggy, and Unity is slooooow. It seems more like a down-grade than an upgrade in all honesty.

It'll stay on my 'travelling companion' and be very welcome there, but *no way* will it go back on my desktop machine.

Nor am i at all confident about PP any more, and will not try it unless it is a lot more polished and stable than OO.

Come April I will be replacing 10.10 with 10.04, just so i can keep support for something that 'works' for a bit longer.

### Post by cariboo on 2012-01-25
I personally think that Ubuntu became so easy to use around the release of Lucid, that many of us have become complacent, and assume that everything in Unity would be just as easy as the old two panel interface. Most of the complaints I've seen here, are easily fixed by spending a few hours searching for and applying the many customizing techniques available for Unity. 

Keep in mind that the Gnome 2 panel interface was around for 9 years, and at it's start it was just as hard to use as Unity is now. I've been using Unity since the pre-alpha release at the start of the Maverick development cycle, what we had then, and what we have now is 100% better, and with the improvements made so far in Precise, even though it's only at alpha 1, most of the buginess is gone, and it's quite stable.

There has been a lot of work going on creating test suites to cover testing of all aspects of the interface, the last update to Unity even included a reporting applet that ran the user through a test suite, and reported back to the developers.

If you would like to help test the next release, that will be supported for the next 5 years, effenberg0x0 and I will be holding a session during Ubuntu Developer Week on running a testing release. Just show up on irc at #ubuntu-classroom on irc.freenode.net on Feb 1/2012 at 18:00 UTC. We'll answer as many questions as possible during the ½ hour session.

### Post by Double.J on 2012-01-26
Hi there! sorry to hear you don't like it... it has taken a while to get used to for me too. I actually quite like it now. My only bugbear is that it's hard to find things you don't know are there.

The trouble is that the gnome interface you are referring to is dead :( That was gnome 2. this has been replaced by gnome 3, sadly we can't have both, but that's life. Gnome 3 is a bit more complicated - we've got gnome "shell" as the default user interface of gnome now. The idea is that the shell is the basis, and different distros/3rd party projects build their own user interface from this.. this is what unity is. If you go back to gnome shell it will actually seem more like ubuntu's unity to you than gnome 2 sorry :(

As whatthefunk says, there's always kubuntu, lubuntu and xubuntu, and if you don't mind the doubling up of apps you can add them to your current install?

You may also be interested in 2 projects: 
[MATE]("http://matsusoft.com.ar/projects/mate/")
[Cinnamon]("http://cinnamon.linuxmint.com/")

MATE is a fork of gnome 2 designed to keep it going, and cinnamon is a fork of gnome shell, designed to provide a more tradtitional menuing system on gnome 3. Bare in mind both are still very new projects :)

---

I am sorry that threads like this are slowly taken over by people who don't read, listen or intend to learn, nor to make things work. For almost all problems that are mentioned here, there are numerous solutions, proposed numerous times by numerous people in numerous threads. It takes two parties to make things work.


I am sorry for this grumpy post. Happens to me now and then...

I am quite tired of people who just wont listen to others and yet want to defend their own opinions as if theirs is the only one

Then to tell people to get over it is quit belittling.

I could say the same thing, People don't like, get over it, create something that people like which is the community of Linux to beging with.

Dont' try to forcefeed them something in the name of change where this change is actually for the worst and yet some or few may have an opinion that it's good simply because it has the name "CHANGE" attached to it.

Change for the worst is still bad and Unity is still illogical method of browsing for applications and creates a lot of work.

This type of hierarchy of browsing for applications has already been perfected and logically so.

Creating some new method of interface simply because it's new and different doesn't mean it works any better or has better functionality.

Again as I pointed out previously are websites going to switch to this method of trying find something on their site by learning a new shortcut key for each site that desired to try something new.
This would be foolishness.
There is a rhyme and reason for the logistics that led us to this type of point and click browse menus type of system that everyone has grown to love. It's the simplicity and user friendliness of it all.

Why can't people listen and understand all this. 
We could change to a simply NON interface altogether and simply let people use the console but thats not change for the best it's change for the worst. But to attempt to force feed into submission on this topic is just silliness talking points.

Again logic is logic regardless and we can't simply CHANGE outsided of the scope of logic and user friendliness no matter how pleasant the visual effects might look.

I gave Unity an honest try. I really wanted to make it work. for a few good months. And believe me I use all my computers daily for my business.

Fact is it simply doesn't work well and is not a logical inteface for users.
I constantly have to tell people how to get around, then can't find things on their own that exist on the system.
Then can't get to certain things that would otherwise be as simply as browsing the menu and saying AH HA there it is.

Memorizing shortcuts is not the answer.

And getting rid of the appility to open multiple instances across desktops is not good either.

It needs some serious improvements before I can even use it.
Currently I can't open java apps and charts on one desktop while opening firefox on a second desktop. I believe this is by design and not a bug.

Networking .gvfs or hidden folders don't stay open like they use to. Lets say you want to attach a file for yahoo mail or even list an image that you have on a share or server location.

.gvfs is hidden so you have to alway always go to home folder first, then ctrl+h to unhide the .gvfs folder and the finally browse to your bookmarks or network share always.

Because the bookmarked created network share doesn't work with the java applications that wants you to browse for your attached file or ebay image listing. 
So you can't use your bookmark cause it doesn't work so you have to browse deep into the file system but first you have to unhide the gvsf folder first.

So this means that your going to do a lot of clicking around, however this is not a Unity specific issue but likely a Gnome issue however combined with all the Unity issues this all adds up to a HUGE mess.

SMB4K use to solve this topic and worked well but is broken on Untuntu for quite some time now since about version 9.04 or about.

If you want to save an image or webpage click firefox and save-as same thing all over again lots of clicking around to do, 

It's workin me man it's workin me to death and I won't use it anymore. I can't it's not logical to do so. It is way too much work.

However I will say this for simply browsing for someone who only wants to get on the internet and check email and maybe facebook only it's probably great.

The list goes on and it's too long to actually discuss in one topic but overal performance and user friendlyness i would say a poor rating.

If this was Ebay I would have to give negative feedback on this one.

I do enjoy the Gnome shell since I've switch all these computers back over from Unity to Gnome classic (no effects) for speed as well;  and the compatibility and user friendliness we all have come to know and love but it does leave me somewhat wanting as well.

Overall a many have stated I have to give Unity a sort of 2 stars our of 5 rating

In spite of all this I'm still trying to make it work because I don't want to customize and reloading systems I've always enjoyed using all the Ubuntu defaults as they stand.

Sure makes installation a breeze, but I just can't seem to get around some of these problems and the logic of it all.

Happy blogging, sorry to have such a negative outlook on Unity but it just does not work for many many people as they have expressed.
If it was so good peope would be saying this overwhelmingly with positive ratings and feedback.

To assume people are reluctant to change in a Linux community is sort of an oximoron as well.
I know for sure that people will always embrace change for the best, and not change for the worst it's sort of elementary which which is why the use Linux to begin with.

### Post by JC Cheloven on 2012-01-27
Hi, I don't post often to this kind of threads, but want to make an exception. 

After wondering for moths about the purpose of Unity, to which I find it difficult to get used to, I think windows8 opened my eyes. As I see it now, the purpose is that the average user in the years to come can use and see the same interface in his pc, his phone, his tablet, ...etc. 

Thus, the definition of what a user finds "intuitive" is being (or will be) redefined. Unity is intended to give a position to canonical in particular and to gnu-linux in general in that new scenario.

From this point of view, I think Unity is a good move. The price of the bet will be that hard pc-users will prefeer a specialized and productive interface (say xfce, lxde). It happens to be that current gnu-linux users fall for the most part in this category, and that's why so many *curent* users don't like it. 

What do you think?

---

### Post by Jacobonbuntu on 2012-01-27

> Hi, I don't post often to this kind of threads, but want to make an exception.

Thus, the definition of what a user finds "intuitive" is being (or will be) redefined. Unity is intended to give a position to canonical in particular and to gnu-linux in general in that new scenario.

From this point of view, I think Unity is a good move. The price of the bet will be that hard pc-users will prefeer a specialized and productive interface (say xfce, lxde). It happens to be that current gnu-linux users fall for the most part in this category, and that's why so many *curent* users don't like it. 

What do you think?

I think it is an interesting thought, but I don't believe it is true, and to my opinion not giving Unity what it deserves. I really, really do not want to underestimate the traditional Ubuntu user, but I am quite surprised by the current critic from that corner. Before I switched to Linux I always had the image of Linux (and Ubuntu-) users that they were handy, and master over their system.  It turns out not to be like that. 

As I said somewhere before, in my opinion the phase Unity is in now is that it is a good product, but not completely finished (yet). Advanced users can easily "finish" it with the help of a few relatively simple tools or changes like Compiz, My Unity, quicklists(!), editing fstab for quick acces to network locations etc. etc. Clicking a lot and other complaints that we see, is really not necessary with a good "finished" installation. Again, to my surprise a lot of the traditional users seem not to manage. 

I would suggest people ask for help rather than complaining. Reading almost identical posts in this section (and where not on these forums?), while answers/solutions were proposed endlesly in the help section or elsewhere on the internet, is not an inviting experience.

### Post by HDave on 2012-01-27

> Hi, I don't post often to this kind of threads, but want to make an exception. 

After wondering for moths about the purpose of Unity, to which I find it difficult to get used to, I think windows8 opened my eyes. As I see it now, the purpose is that the average user in the years to come can use and see the same interface in his pc, his phone, his tablet, ...etc. 

Thus, the definition of what a user finds "intuitive" is being (or will be) redefined. Unity is intended to give a position to canonical in particular and to gnu-linux in general in that new scenario.

It happens to be that current gnu-linux users fall for the most part in this category, and that's why so many *curent* users don't like it. 

What do you think?

Interesting viewpoint.  I've been a harsh critic of Unity, and my previous posts have explained in detail why.  However, I agree with you that Unity is the right move...and is taking Ubuntu in the right direction.  I am a Linux power user, yet I can say I love the idea of Unity.  It is just that the current implementation of Unity is SO half baked...so partially thought through...its frankly embarrassing.

I think Unity is damaging Ubuntu's otherwise excellent reputation amoung experienced desktop users, especially ones with large monitors that do not want to trade usability for screen real estate.  It's a shame that Gnome couldn't have held off on killing Gnome2 for another 1-2 years...by that time Unity would have been ready for prime time and the transition would have been smoother and more exciting.

But I think you are right that it is moving in the correct direction.  I have filed or voted all of my bugs in Unity...and I know its only a matter of time at this point which is why I, for one, am going to stick with Ubuntu until Unity is ready -- though I'm only going to run Ubuntu 10.04 or Xubuntu 11.10 for now.

### Post by ctilluma on 2012-01-29
I don't post often, as evidenced by my post count. I felt though as if I needed to post here regarding Unity. I maintain a number of laptops, workstations, and servers for myself, friends, and family. 

Up until recently, the systems I recommend and support for friends and family had been Mac's. I enjoy the power of the backend while users enjoy the relatively intuitive ease of use of Aqua. The servers I maintain have all migrated from various flavors of Linux over the years to Ubuntu, while my laptop had until recently ran OSX. I have had problems with Apple's making it difficult with certain aspects such as FLAC over the years, but until Lion the incentives to change weren't present. Numerious changes to Lion and recent licensing aspects have laid the proverbial straw on the camel.

So I went back to Ubuntu for my main system. Imagine my surprise when I saw Unity instead of Gnome2. While it took a moment to get accustomed to the interface, I have been nothing but pleased with it. It has significantly increased my productivity and pleasure with amazing intuition, wonderful shortcuts, and reduced distractions allowing me to get where I need quickly and get the work done without a mess of clutter. I still have tremendous power accessing less frequently used apps using a minute number of keystrokes and when I really want I can get into terminal for full fun. While I have moved terminal to my dashboard, enabling me to get there in a single keystroke, even if I hadn't done so, it only takes 4 keys to bring it up. I am overwhelmingly happy with what a great job has been done on Unity over Gnome or KDE, and will actually considering running a GUI on some of my servers now that have the resources. I will be migrating all the systems I support that are not Ubuntu/Unity over, since not only does it provide more power in a far more customize able manner than Macs or PC's, but when queried with friends and family, many IT pros of different flavors and many basic end users, it does so in a format that exceeds previous interfaces (Aqua, Windows, Gnome, KDE) in usability.

In fact, the interface is so usable and intuitive, then when I reloaded my 4 year old's netbook today from Windows 7 to Ubuntu 11.10, his eyes lit up bigger than I've seen them with a plethora of gifts for the holidays. Having never experienced linux (only the mac's and his windows system), he was able to navigate and find everything he needed. He doesn't even have to spell the whole word of an application when looking for it, he just brings up search and sounds out the beginning and voila, there is the app he wants.

Simple, easy, intuitive and powerful. A perfect storm for a winning combination. For the first time with linux (and i've been using it non-stop since the late 90's), I have a primary system that truly gives me the power and flexibility I crave with a simple interface that makes accessing what I need to when I need to in the quickest, easiest manner possible.

Menu'd systems, with a series of drop down menu's are a thing of the past. They are unnecessary clutter and limit the ability to access in a linear fashion. Unfortunately, not every bodies mind thinks in the same linear fashion, and a linear fashion prevents access in more than one fashion. First we lost linear physical data storage methods (and i was very glad to stop using tape drive), replaced with random access for quicker finding of data while abstracting less necessary information, such as the exact starting point on a storage media. Then we lost linear data access methods, such as databases - again providing greater ability to query data while further abstracting aspects of information. With the advent of tags and tagging systems, we are phasing out linear file storage methods. (Which makes tremendous sense as well. One may want to access a tax return based on the year, a finance keyword, tax, or any other number of keywords. The idea of only accessing a file based on it's path was limiting and is finally on it's way out. We have further increased the ability to access the exact information needed while abstracting what is now less necessary information to the user, the drive tree. We have now pushed another layer into abstraction, providing a greater ability to quickly access information and processes, this time further up the stack into the application layer. Usability has now taken the plunge. No longer do users have to worry about where down some series of menus to find an application or file. If it's accessed frequently, it's right there on the bar. If it isn't it usually isn't more than 4 to 6 keystrokes away. Without having to spend the cycles to have to remember information or hunt it down, a significantly greater productivity can be realized. It's not like many people still memorize phone numbers, having to type the whole number in each time making a call. A couple key presses, and the contact gets dialed. No longer having to waste memory on 10 digit numbers, no longer having to expend the cycles with each key press. It enables users to get the job done more efficiently, mentally and time wise. Yes, you can still memorize the full number, and yes, you can still type out the whole thing, but in most non critical cases, why would you want to? And for those critical cases, memorize the number but use the faster method unless your contacts aren't accessible.

I have never been happier with a release than to discover Unity. For power users such as myself all the way to brand new end users.It's amazing, wonderful and fantastic. In 30 years of computing, this advancement is one of the few to so substantially increase productivity without decreasing capabilities.

Thank you Canonical for such a tremendous vision and decision in moving forward with Unity.

---

### Post by ctilluma on 2012-01-29
> **cmcanulty said:**
> Here is a specific example that I figured out but is unnecessarily obtuse.In system settings-user accounts if you add an account it says disabled and then if you cl on password you can enter one and then it enables. But it should ask to establish a password so someone sees the option, now unless you click around it is-why is this account disabled. ... I could list hundreds of examples but each one require more clicks, more knowledge than the classic versions. Yet unity is supposed to be easier for new users or non techie types, when in fact the opposite is true. Of course all this could be cleaned up but for now it is unacceptable.

I find it to be extremely intuitive. You click a + to add a new user and enter the details. User is created. You see the user is disabled, and when you click on disabled (because where else would make intuitive sense to click if you wanted to enable someone, other than disabled?) then you are presented with a set password dialog. Fill it out, and it's done. User is created and enabled. Very few clicks, no mucking around - just a clean, intuitive interface, one to create and one to enable. Requires no knowledge for doing so, whether a techie or not. Clean and simple. Broken into two pieces intentionally, for ease of use and security. There are plenty of users I create that handle system processes, that don't need to be enabled. They don't need passwords or to be able to log in. I can create the process users in the least amount of effort and with the least amount of unnecessary clutter. Exactly what is needed for a system account. As for those users who do get to log in, a simple click on the one spot that makes the most sense - disabled. Enter the password and it's done. Again, no extra hoopla, what is needed to get the job done quickly and easily so I can focus my efforts on more complex tasks, such as service integrations and configurations.

---

### Post by AgentZ86 on 2012-02-03
There are too many negative aspects of Unity interface that can't be overcome but simple repairs. The logistics of the interface is all completely wrong to begin with

Who decided to put the panel to the left, along with the window buttons on the left (close,minimize,maximize) everytime you go to the left the stupid panel pops out in front of the buttons that you are trying to access. (illogical interface).

Thats just the tip of the iceberg but the complete interface needs reworking.
It's all been said and pointed out in this forum but again. Top panel can't be hidden without compiz-settings-manager etc.(illogical)

Can't access open windows without memorizing some shortcuts.(illogical)
Can't find existing applications easily without searching a Dash lense or clicking the filtered menus (illogical and bulky)

Power menu is inaccessible or disappears while other windows or application windows are open (illogical interface)

Hidden files once unhidden and browsed to that location for use of perhaps a server or shared file, automatically rehide themself, making it very very hard to browse a network or shared recourse on a regular basis. Deep into the heirarchy folder structure browsing is a real pain. Creating a shortcut to that location partially works but not in most applications. Especially if you want to attach a file to your webmail or yahoo mail etc. These bookmarks don't work so you have to unhide, then browse deep into the file system again. (illogical, illogical, illogical interface)

Although it looks nice, and it's new but it's certainly NOT better or more logical. It's worse and impractical especially for new users who expect to browse and search around for stuff on their computer easily. NOT to memorize shortcuts.
Thats just the way people are, they want something they can sit down to and click around and experience the programs not something they have to read a bunch of help menus to find out what shortcuts are and how to get around.

I'm not really comlaining for myself but for others who simply want to click their found programs and use them.

I know they will not be able to use the computer the way that I do and that they will never learn these commands. Some can hardly click the menus already and barely understand that there are separate windows and can minimize things.
Anything past this will not be good for these types of new users and believe me there are still many who may come from windows environment and thats all they know.

Will this attract them if it's intimidating to them or will it send them back to windows ? Perhaps that the plan I can't say but it's just the way people are and the way it is.

People want to talk about change throughout this post but not willing to really change anything to meet the needs of the people they simply want to change the people.
Why not change and create something that people wanted or desired instead of trying to mold them into your OS

Oh well doubtful that will happen, which is why we have this political mess around the world. Perfect example here.

### Post by craig10x on 2012-02-08
I wonder if it has ever occurred to the ubuntu developers that adding the following configuration options for unity would potentially make it so much more pleasant to work with?

1) Auto Hide Top Panel Option
2) Turn off "global menu" option
3) Move buttons on top of window from left to traditional right hand side option
4) Move Unity Panel to bottom and reduce it's width option (so it's more compact like the Cairo Dock)

I heard that 12.04 will include "MyUnity" which will enable one to make some changes, but if the above were included, i think Unity would get a much wider acceptance...don't you agree? :)

That way...those who like unity in stock form can use it as such, but for many others, this would greatly broaden it's appeal!

I wish one of the developers would see this and suggest it...

### Post by BigSilly on 2012-02-15
Right, so I've had a good play with 12.04 on a USB, just running live to see how it feels and look at the changes, and I am now utterly convinced that the decision to make the Unity bar always visible by default is the best solution. I was really strongly against the idea at first, believing that it would just take up too much screen space. I've been using 11.10 up until this point with the dodge windows function that is to be removed from 12.04. However, just reducing the size of the Unity icons a wee bit makes such a big difference, and I love the bar being always on now. I only have a 19" monitor yet it looks and works brilliantly.

It's funny how the forums were full of people complaining about the bar popping up unwanted all the time, but when the solution is offered they didn't like that either! I've now set my 11.10 install to run with the bar always on, and am really much, much happier with it this way.

Cannot wait for Precise! :D

### Post by 0011235813 on 2012-02-17
One of the most annoying things I find about DEs is the annoying and irrational bashing of the Unity DE. I like Unity, there, I said it. That said, I actually prefer Gnome 3.2 as my favorite DE. What I don't understand is why people constantly rant against it;
"Oh, I hate Unity! I am so going to change distro!"
Really, I can't find anything wrong with it (okay, maybe switching between apps in the Launcher Bar could be sped up a little), but that's just my personal opinion. If you don't like it... Whoa! There's no need to jump ship! 

In the login screen, above where you write in your password, there's a little spanner. If you click on that, you will be given a list of interfaces to choose from. 

If you don't have KDE (not that I can understand why people like it so much, but hey), you can install it with:
```
sudo apt-get install kde-desktop-environment
```
which just installs the interface, to install the full desktop (Rekonq and Amarok etc) you can use:
```
sudo apt-get install kubuntu-desktop
```
*WARNING! Large download*

For LXDE:
```
sudo apt-get install lxde
```

I think Gnome is installed by default, though I'd have to check.

PS: If you want to change quickly from Unity to Gnome, type:
```
gnome-shell --replace
```
in the terminal (Ctrl+Alt+T). 
*WARNING! This does not work well with other DEs, though it might make a good backup in case something goes wrong*

### Post by Myrddin Emrys on 2012-02-18
> **Snocrash said:**
> You say it is a dead product no longer supported. Fine. I believe the code is available. Build it and give us an optional version with Gnome 2.

Someone already did:

[http://mate-desktop.org/](http://mate-desktop.org/)
[http://wiki.mate-desktop.org/download#ubuntu](http://wiki.mate-desktop.org/download#ubuntu)

I generally just add it to a standard Ubuntu installation and keep Unity around as an alternative desktop, but you can install MATE as the only DE if you prefer:

[http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?p=11696611#post11696611](http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?p=11696611#post11696611)

Either way, it justs adds a few minutes to your installation time.

### Post by exploder on 2012-02-19
I have been testing Ubuntu 12.04 64 bit out on my new HP laptop because it supports my ralink WiFi out of the box. Prior to this, my only experience with Unity was on Oz Unity, (the first release). I liked Oz Unity because it was different and I mean in a cool way.

I think that people completely miss the point of Unity. Ubuntu wants to be competitive and profitable and the old desktop environments we know and love just have not got very far with consumers. 

Just take cell phones as an example. I see people rush out and spend $300.00 or more just to have the latest greatest phone with a cool new interface. Are the new cell phones easier to use? No. There is always a learning curve but people are really quick to adapt because they are anxious to show off that shiny new phone. Google Android sure illustrates this trend.

Unity is trying to do the same thing in a way. Unity is trying to achieve cool new ways of doing the same old tasks. Unity is not for everyone though, just like some people still want a very basic cell phone that is simple to use. 

Ubuntu is trying to make things fresh and cool across a wide range of devices. Apple took this route and look at the success they are enjoying for not putting all their eggs in one basket. Ubuntu's TV interface really looks great and they are coming up with new ways and terminology to make things more unique. 

The idea and the terminology behind "Lenses" is actually a brilliant move if you think about it. This is a terrific marketing idea! Ubuntu has been addressing the things that people that are not familiar with Linux have had problems understanding. The Software Center and the way new packages are being offered when you might need them is a very good idea. When I went to try out the webcam on my laptop, I was asked if I would like to install Cheese. This is exactly what someone that was not familiar with Linux distributions would need to help them out getting started.

Pressing the super key to see a list of keyboard shortcuts is a great idea too and it seemed really handy because I did not know any of them. The Me menu is really handy too and I am glad that it is still included and maintained. The way chat and mail are integrated into the menu is original and it adds to the cool factor. 

I like the way I can change the size of the icons in the Unity launcher. I like the icons smaller than default but some people with eyesight problems would be grateful for the larger icons. I noticed that icons in general throughout the entire system are more consistent in size, consistency is a good thing and it gives polish to Unity.

Think about all of the choices that are available since Gnome 3 was introduced. You have, Mate, Cinnamon, Gnome Shell, Gnome fallback mode, Unity 3D, Unity 2D and I am sure others will spring up. There is pretty much something for everyone and more ways to customize things with add-ons and extensions. All of these things are new and they will evolve and get better over time, just look at how fast things have progressed already!

I think Unity has a huge edge over the new Windows 8 interface too. Canonical has been doing usability studies and they are looking at new ideas from Ubuntu Brainstorm and this has helped to make things better. Most of the complaints about Unity have come from people that are used to a traditional desktop setup that's roots can be traced back to the Windows 95 interface. Consumers are ready for something fresh and new and Canonical has seen that putting a new coat of paint on an old desktop environment is not what consumers are looking for.

I learned clear back in high school that the businesses that are highly profitable are the ones that are willing to take the highest risks, this is what Canonical is doing. Mark Shuttlelworth is after all a businessman and has invested his time and money to have a profitable Linux company. RedHat has demonstrated that you can be profitable with Linux but what works for them does not necessarily make it the business model for everyone. 

People complained that Ubuntu was just using Debian's work before. Why are people complaining when Ubuntu is trying to do something original completely on their own and using their own ideas? Ubuntu holds quite a few events that squash bugs that do give back to Debian and Debian uses the Software Center in their releases, so Ubuntu does impact Linux in a positive way. 

Consider all of these things before you start bashing Unity. There are choices available if you desire a traditional desktop, they are right there in the Ubuntu repos. Also take into consideration what consumers want that have no experience of any kind with Linux systems.

---

12.04 is running so nice i am tempted to install now, but will probably wait for the final release ;)

### Post by exploder on 2012-02-19
Thanks craig10x, Jacobonbuntu!

Some other things to think about. People complained that they did not like gdm because it was not very easy to customize. Ubuntu started using LightDM and look at how easy it is to change the background on the greeter now. LightDM uses the users wallpaper and it does it automatically, that's a pretty nice feature.

People complained about mono being included and Ubuntu is doing away with it in 12.04. The installer now gives you the ability to install proprietary drivers for your graphics card, this was a very good move that makes things so much easier for people that have no Linux experience. Codecs can be installed right from the installer now too and I know a lot of people that could not grasp how to do this in earlier releases.

My biggest problem with Ubuntu has always been with graphics drivers for my machines. I finally realize that the LTS releases are going to have the best hardware support because of their target audiences and 12.04 seems to have excellent hardware support so far. It may just be good timing but even the open source drivers are looking very good. My laptop is using ATI graphics and the open source drivers are giving me the default Unity desktop with the associated desktop effects.

Ubuntu switched it's default e-mail application to Thunderbird a while back, this was a welcome change for most of us. Thunderbird is actively developed and at least in my experience, Evolution was always pretty buggy with the same bugs being present in every release.

The Unity launcher in my opinion is a good idea, so many people like the idea of using a dock for their most used applications and Ubuntu has added some handy right click options to the Unity launcher. Sure the hiding option could use some re-thinking but the developers are aware of this and who knows what the future will hold for it.

HUD is an interesting idea that seems to have captured some interest. People complain that Ubuntu dumbs things down too much but HUD is in no way a dumbing down feature. Not everyone will like or use HUD but for those that do use it, it could be pretty nice and you have to admit it has originality.  

Unity is distancing itself from the traditional desktop and imagine the hurdles that have to be overcome to come up with something original and being able to apply the technology to a wide variety of devices. Ubuntu is not trying to come up with a Windows or Mac OS clone, they are trying to define themselves on their own merits wile providing something consumers would desire. 

Microsoft made history when they introduced Windows 95 because at the time it was fresh and new, this appears to be what Canonical would like to do with Unity.     

I expect to see some very major changes coming in future Ubuntu releases. I don't see Canonical wanting to be a situation where they would be forced to pay Microsoft or anyone else when they release new products and I would bet that they make certain that no one has any kind of chance of winning any lawsuits that may be filed against them. Current patent laws are killing competition and you can tell by the way Canonical is doing things that they are protecting themselves from this kind of crap and that's good for all of us.

---

### Post by craig10x on 2012-02-19

2) like the new options (like reducing the icon size in the unity bar)  one option i hope they will include soon (though i know it won't be on 12.04) is the option to place the unity bar on the bottom...but in a more compact width size (one would not want it to stretch the whole length of the bottom of the screen...perhaps more like Cairo Dock in width)...

   That would be a nice option for those of us that would like to have it on the bottom...

Also i would like to say that i like the global menu because of the extra screen space it gives you (especially when using the web browser)  :)

And, you're welcome exploder....great post

### Post by exploder on 2012-02-19
I see people going through a learning curve with new cell phones all the time and not minding it, the same is true with Unity. People that are new to Linux have no idea of what programs they need because they do not know what they are called but when the operating system itself brings up choices that is really something. My wife could not grasp package management in Linux but Ubuntu has taken away that learning curve with how they are presenting new software to the end user.

Displaying top rated software right in the Dash is a great way of showing people what is available and it helps people to understand package management. 

The options in the Me menu help to organize the common tasks that most people use and they do not have to dig through menu layers to find the things that they want to do right off the bat, it is handy,looks cool and makes it easy for new users to get started.

The battery options for mobile devices is an awesome feature! The ability to display how much time you have left on a battery and the state of the battery is awesome! Having this ability right in the panel is a great feature.

Unity is evolving to be pretty nice because it can cater to a new users needs and still have features like HUD that appeal to advanced users. Unity started out as a very basic idea but as newer versions are released more refinements, new ideas and more advanced technology are appearing. It just takes time to try and re-invent an operating system that consumers will want.

I have noticed that with 12.04 much more information is right out in the open in the development forum. It is really easy to see what is going on with things.

I actually liked how much Unity was progressing so much that I removed the stock Windows 7 that came with my new laptop and installed a daily build of 12.04. Windows 7 was boring and it was not a very enjoyable experience using the laptop. The daily build I installed with Unity was anything but boring and I find myself using the laptop a lot more than I was before.

Unity seems much easier to navigate on the laptop because it is so much easier to see on the smaller screen. Try reading the "all applications" section of the Windows 7 menu on a smaller screen while sitting on your couch, it's really hard to read the entries! Unity with the default theme is very easy to see and text stands out making things very easy to read. It's the like this that go a long way.

My wife bought an Android tablet when I was in the hospital, we never use it... I do not like the UI on the tablet at all, it is in no way user friendly to me. For me Unity would be a better choice even on the tablet.

Time will tell if Unity is heading in the right direction but in my opinion it is the best attempt to re-invent the desktop that has come along so far.

Edit: I just tried out the new Video Lens that made it into Unity 5.4, nice! This is the type of feature that really justifies having a learning curve with a new UI. Things like this would appeal to consumers.

---

Just type "Edit photos" and something like GIMP should be suggested by HUD. In that respect it is aimed at novice users. It's not aimed at advanced users because they know where to find GIMP anyway without the HUD.

> **exploder said:**
> I actually liked how much Unity was progressing so much that I removed the stock Windows 7 that came with my new laptop and installed a daily build of 12.04. Windows 7 was boring and it was not a very enjoyable experience using the laptop. The daily build I installed with Unity was anything but boring and I find myself using the laptop a lot more than I was before.

Maybe you were lucky, because there are many laptops that don't work at all smoothly with Ubuntu (graphics/wifi driver issues). To my mind Ubuntu would be wise to fix such basic hardware related issues once and for all instead of playing with a new desktop interface. If Unity hadn't been conceived, nobody would miss it. But if your WiFi does not work, you would miss it...

The main problem with Android is auto-maximizing of windows, and not being able to kill apps easily, feels very clumsy. Another problem is its ties with Google services i.e. datamining. That's why I would like Unity on my tablet, but NOT on my desktop. I'm not interested in a uniform interface across devices, there will always be devices with yet another interface.

### Post by exploder on 2012-02-20
I noticed a couple of people mention poor hardware support in Ubuntu. I have to admit that for me hardware support was horrible on anything after 8.04. Hardware support for the graphics cards in two of my computers was terrible with most distribution too though, with the exception of PCLinuxOS. My ATI and NVidea graphics cards worked perfectly with PCLinuxOS. Needless to say PCLinuxOS KDE has been the operating system of choice on my main computer for nearly a year now and I have no plans of ever using anything else on this machine.

My laptop is another story though. The laptop has a Ralink WiFi that is not yet supported under PCLinuxOS and the laptop will not be used as much. Under a rolling release it is necessary to keep the system updated frequently to prevent breakage. I tried the Ubuntu 12.04 alpha release because I was reasonably sure that the WiFi would be supported with the newer kernel and it was!

I did try the Live CD on my main desktop and the open source NVidea drivers worked with no freezing, so there has been quite a bit of work done with both the open source ATI and NVidea drivers.

I see very few complaints about drivers so far in Ubuntu 12.04. Maybe it is just good timing for the LTS but when I see desktop effects working on two machines with open source drivers and no system freezes it is very encouraging. 

I tried out the new "Video Lens" in Unity 5.4 yesterday and I must say that it is a brilliant idea and feature on Ubuntu's part. What other OS has this feature? None! Things like this put Unity in a class by itself because it is new, cool and it makes the idea of Lenses shine. It was a great marketing move using the term "Lens" too because it is a unique name for what they have developed and I think it could be a catchy thing with consumers.

The whole idea of Lenses opens up the door for more add-ons and it is a safe bet that more Lenses will be developed and introduced over time. 

The way additional software is introduced to the new user is excellent also. I like how software that can be installed is presented in the applications lens and in the Software Center itself. People that are new to Linux have no idea just how much software is available and they are not familiar with the names of the applications they might want. The way Ubuntu is putting software out in the open for new users goes a long way on getting them started and enjoying open source.

I see a lot of good things going on with 12.04 and if things keep heading in this direction I can see Canonical tuning a good profit.

### Post by hzFVOW00pw on 2012-02-21
> **exploder said:**
> I see very few complaints about drivers so far in Ubuntu (12.04)

Of course, not many people install it because it's alpha now.

Hardware problems I have at he moment:

Proprietary NVidia: Ugly plymouth boot screen. This is a perfect example of a feature (plymouth) that should have been better tested before it was integrated in Ubuntu. After all, the first popup after a fresh Ubuntu install is about installing proprietary drivers. This bug is about 2 years old, and never fixed.

Problem with Nouveau driver: No dual monitor support. I can't live without dual monitors because I have my TV hooked up. This is a feature that has been working for ages under Windows.

Also, a friend of mine has an NVidia Optimus chipset, he's in for a tough ride to get it working with the Bumblebee code or Ironhide PPA. At the moment there are no packages for Precise.

Then there's a Canon printer and scanner from a friend of mine that both do not work in Ubuntu.

I understand that the problem is mostly with closed source drivers, and I don't know the solution, but still, people will definately be put off by such issues...

This is why I don't really care about the new DE's and just stick with good old XFCE. Maybe Linux developers should make more effort to communicate with proprietary software builders? Or put double the effort in reverse engineering? Isn't that more needed than flashy desktops?

---

I was given a Kodak Hero 7.1 printer as a gift last October, and until recently it has been a large paperweight as far as Ubuntu was concerned.  But, suddenly, almost out of nowhere, a driver for this printer has been developed.  THAT is what we want from Ubuntu, not pretty graphics.

### Post by exploder on 2012-02-21
Ubuntu or any distribution has very little control over device drivers, they pretty much give you what is available. Some time ago I had a nice Lexmark printer/scanner that would not work with Linux, I just traded it for an HP. 

As far as a pretty interface goes, Canonical is gaining more and more partners all the time because of it. I just read today about the Ubuntu desktop on a new Android phone, that's pretty slick!

[http://www.omgubuntu.co.uk/2012/02/ubuntu-for-android-announced/](http://www.omgubuntu.co.uk/2012/02/ubuntu-for-android-announced/)

I would have never imagined Ubuntu being used in that fashion on a cell phone! This is a really creative idea and lets not forget about Ubuntu going on TVs either. Apple thought about more than just the desktop to make record profits and it looks like Canonical is going with the same approach, it makes good business sense. 

The more popular Linux gets, the more likely you will see more drivers appearing. If manufacturers want to sell their hardware to companies building Linux powered devices they will invest time in providing decent drivers.

Linux missed the boat on netbooks and I don't think Canonical wants to see anything like that happen again. Canonical is not putting all their eggs in one basket so to speak and that is a very smart thing from a business point of view. 

Canonical is really showing some creativity lately. I always complained that there was too many articles about Ubuntu that really did not amount to anything but this is not the case now, these guys are getting somewhere and it's nice seeing Linux finally starting to show up in the mainstream.

Edit: Be sure and watch the video demonstrating the Ubuntu/Android phone, it's an amazing device! The prototype in the video seemed to preform very well and it's capabilities are outstanding!

---

Perhaps Unity will help Linux and Ubuntu reach the big time. Pity I use desktops and don't like Unity. I still need XP on my netbook to update my Satnav and no amount of Unity is going to solve that problem unless millions and millions use Unity.

The phone is interesting and it's a creative idea to say the least. But I have an Android tablet which I can hook up to a keyboard and a HDMI monitor and guess what: I never use that functionality.

Also, it is paired with Android, and thus tied to Google services. That's a big showstopper for me personally (and maybe for more users in the future when people will get more concerned about the datamining practices of Google). When I would buy another phone or tablet, it has to be Ubuntu-only.

The website says "Complete desktop solution for full productivity on docked Android phones". This is a little misleading. I'm sure you can do some lightweigt browsing, but you're not gonna be able to edit video's or photo's on it without significant performance loss as compared to a desktop.

A phone is a device with which you consume data. A desktop computer is a device with which you create data, and do the heavy work. I think over the years desktops will become the tool of choice for the more technically advanced users who can install their own OS and tweak it. Phones and tablets will be consumer/internet devices for the masses, and the masses will mostly leave their desktop. This Ubuntu phone is a hybrid that lacks somewhat as a desktop imo. To me it seems to be neither fish nor fowl.

But then, the future could always prove me wrong, let's hope so :)

---

### Post by exploder on 2012-02-22
Keep in mind that 12.04 is not a final release yet. The work ahead until the final release is centered around fixing bugs. There are bugs that are strictly cosmetic but much of the underlying Gnome packages have not gone final yet.

The basic elements of Unity 5.4 are pretty nice, the features that have been added not only make things more customizable but also adds to the wow factor. The idea of lenses is pretty slick and people in time will be able to add various lenses to suit their different needs. As far as I know no one else has ever tried anything like this, it's simple and unique and it very well could end up being very popular.

Finding ways to depart from the Windows 95 type of traditional desktop and designing something totally fresh is no easy task. I think it all revolves around getting people that have never tried Linux and open source to take notice and see the innovation that free software has to offer.

So many people mistakenly think that Linux should be like a free version of Windows and never really notice the things that make Linux distributions so much better in so many ways. By re-inventing the user interface it better defines things and makes it clear that Linux is not a free Windows or Mac clone.

To me the best example is still modern day cell phones. People rush out to buy the latest greatest cell phone and quickly adapt to the technology. Unity offers the same kind of attraction in my opinion and I think people will quickly adapt to it and on a variety of devices. 

Unity has features built right in that ease the learning curve, just holding the super key gives you all of the keyboard shortcuts as an example. What other operating system has this type of feature built in? The various groups of tasks on the left side of the panel puts everyday things that people need in easy reach and it is very convenient.

In business you either change and improve or get beat by competitors. I think Unity put Canonical at the head of the pack in this respect. I have seen demonstrations of Windows 8 and Microsoft's new user interface and Unity looks like a much more elegant and usable concept to me. The whole idea of Unity combined with the ability to use it on a wide range of products is a very smart move. It just seems like trying to lead the pack is a much better idea than trying to follow what other business do. Canonical wants to be profitable and I think they are taking the right steps to accomplish their goals.

---

You do not need to know the name.

P.S The dash and launcher are just a unity plugin for compiz.
You're free to disable that plugin and use any way you like.

### Post by holytree on 2012-03-11
> **spielball said:**
> How Unity spoiled my experience as a Linux newbie:

1. Unity slows down basic tasks.
In Unity, switching between applications is a pain in the **** when you prefer working with your mouse. Without a task bar and being in a maximized window, one easily loses overview over their open tasks and windows. Super + W is nice when you love working with the keyboard. But it sucks when you love working with the mouse. Unity makes me feel lost on the desktop. And starting an application that I don't use that often, or looking for an app that I need for a specific task (but not knowing if it's installed or not), is just plain dumb in Unity. Why does Unity force me to type into a search box? I can't remember any name of any installed app.

And if I want to see what's installed, I just want to point and click and see an overview. I don't want to be forced to open the Dash, click on the apps category, look in 'Installed Apps', click 'See More', scroll down, etc. It's a pain.

The global menu is basically a good thing. But the way it's implemented, reverses the efficiency into a time-consuming nuisance. I want to 'aim and click', not 'hover, aim and click'.

2. Unity is slow and unstable.
Unity crashes about 5 times per day on my machine. And in about 2-3 cases I have to reboot. This reminds me very much of Windows Explorer back in the 90s.

3. Unity is barely customizable.
I don't want a pre-configured Dash that leaves me no choice. I want to  be able to define what is being shown when I hit the Dash button. I'd  prefer a set of self-defined app icons (shortcuts) instead of a panel  with monster icons that are just wasting spa I don't want a pre-configured Dash that leaves me no choice. I want to   be able to define what is being shown when I hit the Dash button. I'd   prefer a set of self-defined app icons (shortcuts) instead of a panel   with monster icons that are just wasting space. Also, the launcher is   like an unmodifiable brick. When you right-click it, nothing happens.   But that's what would be most intuitive as from the user's perspective.   I'd expect a context menu that offers me to customize it. Instead, I   need CompizConfig and MyUnity to do this. Can it get any more   complicated? The config settings should be part of Unity itself.
ce. Also, the launcher is  like an unmodifiable brick. When you right-click it, nothing happens.  But that's what would be most intuitive as from the user's perspective.  I'd expect a context menu that offers me to customize it. Instead, I  need CompizConfig and MyUnity to do this. Can it get any more  complicated? The config settings should be part of Unity itself.

My conclusion:
Ubuntu became an embarrassment for the Linux world. I had a nice time with 10.04. But 11.10 is unbearable for daily work.

i have a feeling ubuntu 12.04 will solve all those problems

### Post by HDave on 2012-03-12
> **holytree said:**
> i have a feeling ubuntu 12.04 will solve all those problems

I don't think so.  

I [created a launchpad issue for Dash]("https://bugs.launchpad.net/ubuntu/+source/unity/+bug/946019") because I think (as others here have said) that opening apps with the mouse was very unintuitive and overly complex.  The issue was rejected and I was pointed to Ubuntu Brainstorm where I then  [wrote up the issue again]("http://brainstorm.ubuntu.com/idea/29326/").  It was summarily rejected prior to voting because "Unity discusses such improvements internally among participants instead of through Brainstorm"...and I was pointed to [the Unity team's design process]("http://design.canonical.com/2012/02/the-unity-design-process-and-how-you-can-play-a-part-in-it/"), which among other things says that the Unity team design process is open to ideas coming from Ubuntu Brainstorm!! :confused:

At any rate, it is a nice long article that talk about the UNity design process, but doesn't address central issue of it's title...how are we, the community, supposed to participate with launchpad and brainstorm off the table with regard to enhancements.  :-s

The bitter irony here is that the guy who wrote the article killed one of the most (IMO) [reasonable and sought after changes to Unity]("https://bugs.launchpad.net/ayatana-design/+bug/733349"). ](*,)

### Post by Tamlynmac on 2012-03-15
> Hylas de Niall
They only have to check out the Ubuntu web-site to see it and even to try it out (sort of) in their browser.

I use a spare HDD (have both a used internal & external) to install and test all new releases. This assures that it can  be completely tested without any disruption to the existing system. We've tested 8.04 through 11.10 without ever impacting the PC under test in any way.  After deciding 11.04/11.10 weren't for us, we simply rebooted to the primary HDD and investigated alternatives. The nice thing is  we can use the spare HDD to test multiple systems without effecting them.

I learned a long time ago to test any new release or other distro prior  to simply installing it as the primary. All to often bugs, drivers,  etc. or even a failed install can unnecessarily create a frustrated  user. When all one need do is test prior to blindly accepting.

I  recommend testing to every user who wishes to confirm not only  functionality but expectations. I never sympathize with a user who just  assumes it will work, only to complain later. IMHO it indicates a lack of planning. Perhaps the  old theory still applies: If one fails to plan - then one plans to fail. Just a little common sense can go a long way. ](*,)

Just my $0.02

### Post by rima on 2012-03-16
I do like Unity as a whole but there are some things to improve in it. What buggers me really is that you cannot put icons on the background, but ONLY in the left taskbar. Also, when you click on an icon, it does this shiny thing for a while before actually opening the application which really bugs me :/ 

I found that people who got used to Gnome 2 are more likely to complain about Unity than those who have not been introduced to Gnome 2 or similiar before. Just my opinion.

### Post by VH-BIL on 2012-03-16

### Post by stinkeye on 2012-03-16
> **rima said:**
> I do like Unity as a whole but there are some things in it. What buggers me really is that you cannot put icons on the background, but ONLY in the left taskbar. Also, when you click on an icon, it does this shiny thing for a while before actually opening the application which really bugs me :/ 

I can put icons on the desktop here.
If it shows as a .desktop file make it executable.


No launcher animation
```
gconftool-2 --type int --set "/apps/compiz-1/plugins/unityshell/screen0/options/launch_animation" 0
```

set back to default...
```
gconftool-2 --unset "/apps/compiz-1/plugins/unityshell/screen0/options/launch_animation"
```

---

### Post by HDave on 2012-04-02
Been running 12.04 for the past few days and it is certainly an improvement.  Seems more feature rich and stable.  For me, the biggest improvement is that Dash now has "Recent Applications" on the home page and each lens preserves its state -- although Dash still insists on opening to the home page first every time.

Unity 2D seems to be more stable and usable, although it lacks all of the customization that Unity 3D has with the exception of launcher "show/hide".

I am glad Rhythmbox is back and am also glad to see Remmina show up as the default Remote Desktop Protocol viewer....nice.

Bugs still standing in the way for me include:

Unity-3D task switcher is slow to open:
[https://bugs.launchpad.net/ubuntu/+source/compiz/+bug/836023](https://bugs.launchpad.net/ubuntu/+source/compiz/+bug/836023)

Unity-3D optional show-desktop icon button cannot be moved
    [https://bugs.launchpad.net/unity/+bug/932304](https://bugs.launchpad.net/unity/+bug/932304)

unity-2d launcher missing show desktop button (and not in task switcher either)
[https://bugs.launchpad.net/ubuntu/+source/unity/+bug/681348](https://bugs.launchpad.net/ubuntu/+source/unity/+bug/681348)

Empty trash confirmation dialog appears behind maximized windows
     [https://bugs.launchpad.net/ubuntu/+source/unity-2d/+bug/969372](https://bugs.launchpad.net/ubuntu/+source/unity-2d/+bug/969372)

Maximized windows have no window mgt menu pop-up
     [https://bugs.launchpad.net/ubuntu/+source/unity/+bug/969396](https://bugs.launchpad.net/ubuntu/+source/unity/+bug/969396)

Unity click on launcher doesn't minimize app window:
     [https://bugs.launchpad.net/ayatana-design/+bug/733349](https://bugs.launchpad.net/ayatana-design/+bug/733349)

Unity dash should open with same lens as was used last
     [https://bugs.launchpad.net/ubuntu/+source/unity/+bug/946019](https://bugs.launchpad.net/ubuntu/+source/unity/+bug/946019)

Unity launcher hoses mono winforms app icons 
     [https://bugs.launchpad.net/ubuntu/+source/unity/+bug/768931](https://bugs.launchpad.net/ubuntu/+source/unity/+bug/768931)

     Unity workspace switcher icon doesn't indicate what workspace you are in:
[https://bugs.launchpad.net/ayatana-design/+bug/838854](https://bugs.launchpad.net/ayatana-design/+bug/838854)

Overall, I am please to see that things are progressing, although the pace of improvement is still slower than I'd like, the recent improvements to Dash have at least resulted in a usable DE that I can suffer with while they get it to where it needs to be.  My guess is they are still a year away.

This whole ordeal reminds me of KDE 4.0....

---

### Post by shieldw0lf on 2012-04-05
I have a lot of spleen to vent over Unity, but I'm going to try to phrase my concerns in a constructive form.  Here goes.

There's a pervasive attitude that you can immediately pick out, which revolves around using search to interact with your operating system.  

The problem?  Users don't know what their computer can do, what all these programs with the funny names are, or how to find their way around.

The chosen answer?  Make it possible for them to search using common language, based on tags.

Where does this fall down?

It falls down in that it requires you to pay attention, forever.

With previous versions of Ubuntu, everything in the interface was always exactly the way it was last time, every time.  I was so habituated that I could close my eyes and open applications just based on muscle memory.  It didn't disrupt my thought processes in the slightest.

But a search based interface is NEVER going to allow that to happen.  I'm always going to have to look at the search results to make sure I'm clicking on the right thing.  

Even if I get to a point where I can do the necessary clicks and mouse movements and typing to reach Item 2 in the search results, which is usually the one I'm looking for, I never know for sure that a software update isn't going to change that.

When the order of results changes, I can't just rearrange the menu hierarchy to match my habits and get back to work like I used to do.  I need to actually fight my habits, and if I fail because I wasn't paying attention, I need to go through the whole pain in the *** of shutting the application I opened and opening the right one.

This makes the interface is UNLEARNABLE.  It's the same as the way that you can put a bookmark in muscle memory but you can't put a google search result in muscle memory because you have no control over what you get back.

This is a MAJOR ISSUE for anyone who wants to use Ubuntu as a serious tool and not a toy for entertainment, and it goes beyond discussions of which side of the screen the panels ought to be on.  It will never go away with polish.  It is a fundamental.

The search based interface doesn't need to go away, it's useful in it's place, but there NEEDS to be a powerful alternative interface that has hierarchies and is fast, concise and predictable like the old menu's used to be.

Working with the new interface feels like trying to touch type on a keyboard that regularly remaps itself.  It is maddening.

---

### Post by stinkeye on 2012-04-05
Yes, a very academic thesis but I actually find it faster than the old
menu style.
How many applications do you use regularly?
You can fit close to 20 app icons on the launcher?
When looking for seldom used apps in the old menu I spent ages sometimes, especially if I had forgotten the name and it wasn't obvious in what section it would be in.
Using the launcher in combination with kupfer for often used progams
and then the dash search for seldom used programs, I find better to work with than before.
Of course if you've used the gnome2 menu for many years it might seem more logical and faster because by this time you know where every app is.

Over time, in unity you,ll learn what you need to type in to bring that app up first.
I mentioned kupfer earlier. I find it better than both ways of launching programs.
It allows you to "favourite" apps so they will launch with a couple of key presses.

### Post by HDave on 2012-04-05
> **illopos said:**
> I am a 10.04 user thinking about installing 12.04 . I would be very grateful for some guidance on the options and on the issue in general.

I use about 10 apps on every day basis. If I can fit them in a panel like in 10.04 I will be happy. I work with lots of files (not apps) located in different directories very regularly. If I can use unity search to locate them like google desktop it would be very useful to me.

Other than that I run Matlab, use texmaker daily and watch streaming videos. I have a 2 GHZ, 3 GB RAM, ATI 3400 (I think) graphics card laptop. I am wondering if these are enough to run unity for the applications I have mentioned without delays in GUI, which I absolutely hate. I don't mind disabling effects (Unity 2D?).

Also, I have read Gnome classic can be installed with 12.04 . Is it same as the one in 10.04? Will it be maintained regularly?

Finally, what would the battery usage be like compared to 10.04 ?

1. You can fit 10 icons on the launcher no problem.
2. I am not sure if the default Unity search is based on file name, not content, but there are [alternatives]("http://www.webupd8.org/2012/03/recoll-lens-full-text-search-unity-lens.html").  If you want google desktop then just install it.
3. Your memory and CPU are plenty to watch videos and run Unity.
4. I have no idea if you graphics card qualifies you for Unity 3D (based on Compiz), but Unity 2D is finally decent in 12.04.
5. The Gnome classic is not truly the real Gnome 2 as that does not exist anymore except for the piece-of-crap fork called Mate that is pushed within other distros like Mint.  But I have Gnome Classic running in 12.04 and it looks and acts 90% the same.  The app menu is identical.  Just remember to use Super-Alt-RightClick on the Panels.

If you are concerned about speed and performance and memory usage in order to make room for Matlab, and if you really like a traditional desktop, then I strongly urge you try Xubuntu.  I did recently and I have to say that I regret not having run it earlier.  It is clean and in many ways, more stable and functional than Gnome2 ever was.

### Post by Myrddin Emrys on 2012-04-05
> **illopos said:**
> Also, I have read Gnome classic can be installed with 12.04 . Is it same as the one in 10.04? Will it be maintained regularly?

> **HDave said:**
> 5. The Gnome classic is not truly the real Gnome 2  as that does not exist anymore except for the piece-of-crap fork called  Mate that is pushed within other distros like Mint.

Or to put it another way, Gnome 2 exists as as an actively maintained  fork called MATE that is available within other distros like Mint, and also  runs beautifully under Ubuntu. But don't listen to us, give it a try  yourself (a VM is a good way to experiment).

See:

[http://www.howtogeek.com/110052/how-to-install-the-mate-desktop-go-back-to-gnome-2-on-ubuntu/](http://www.howtogeek.com/110052/how-to-install-the-mate-desktop-go-back-to-gnome-2-on-ubuntu/)

[http://mate-desktop.org/](http://mate-desktop.org/)

[http://wiki.mate-desktop.org/download#ubuntu](http://wiki.mate-desktop.org/download#ubuntu)

it might be. it is best to try it out and see how it goes. you can install it next to Ubuntu by installing xubuntu-dekstop or only xfce amnd then select it on login.
 
if you feel satisfied you can remove Gnome : [http://www.psychocats.net/ubuntu/purexfce](http://www.psychocats.net/ubuntu/purexfce)
 
if you don't like it you can get pure gnome back:
[http://www.psychocats.net/ubuntu/puregnome](http://www.psychocats.net/ubuntu/puregnome)
 
note that installing xfce-desktop package will install all additional stuff found in Xubuntu. so you may get 2 battery monitors for example. one left from Gnome and one from Xfce.

### Post by Jacobonbuntu on 2012-04-14
Hi people, a few months ago I started a script to edit quicklists, as an experiment. 
I just finished a GUI version of my quicklist-editor (see below). It should work on both Oneiric and Precise (-beta2). Any comment would be much appreciated!


[CENTER][IMG]http://dl.dropbox.com/u/1155139/editor_window.png[/IMG]

### Post by Jacobonbuntu on 2012-04-23
> **Jacobonbuntu said:**
> Hi people, a few months ago I started a script to edit quicklists, as an experiment. 
I just finished a GUI version of my quicklist-editor (see below). It should work on both Oneiric and Precise (-beta2). Any comment would be much appreciated!


[CENTER] 
[/CENTER]
 
[IMG]http://http//dl.dropbox.com/u/1155139/editor_window.png[/IMG]

you can download it from launchpad[ here]("http://bazaar.launchpad.net/%7Evlijm/jonb.homelauncher/launchermaker/files") (updated almost daily...)
(oops, I linked to outdated files, this one is right, download the QLE.zip)


Hi people, I discovered a potential problem, simpel to solve: the editor creates an initial backup of either the default .desktop file, or (if it exists) of the local one. the editor copied it to *application-name*_initial.desktop. however, multiple .desktop files to the same application can cause mixing up of the icon that is shown in the launcher, so changes you make in the editor have seemingly no effect. it is fixed in the latest version; now the editor names backup files to .txt

please do the following: open the ~/.local/share/applications folder and remove any file with a name ending with "-initial.desktop".

---

### Post by Jacobonbuntu on 2012-06-14
Hi people, just to give an update on the quicklist editor I have been working on the passed few months.
This is a completely new version, -the second "pre-" version-  (see screenshot), re-coded from zero in python 3 now. 
It has a more logical interface, better functionality and a number of build-in precautions to prevent errors. (almost) All editing procedures were totally changed, both at the front-end and behind the screens.

Editing options now include:
- adding commands
- adding dividers
- adding LibreOffice applications (from a list, to any quicklist)
- adding shortcuts to applications (creating grouped quicklists from an application list that is presented)
- adding directories
- adding virtual boxes (from a list that is presented)
- adding url's
- renaming existing entries
- changing the command of an existing entry
- moving entries up or down in the quicklist
- importing entries from textfiles or other quicklists* (experimental)
- removing entries
- resetting the quicklist to as it was before QLE editor was installed

[LEFT]Additionally, the editor recognizes and corrects some possible properties of quicklists that might cause problems (like no ";" at the end of the entry line). The editor recognizes and edits both "old" (<12.04) and "new" style desktop files.
[/LEFT]
[CENTER][LEFT]"standalone" desktop files (made by the user, not representing an application) are recognized and listed, and can be edited like any quicklist.
[/LEFT]


[IMG]https://dl.dropbox.com/u/1155139/editor_window.png[/IMG]

[LEFT]The download link is in my signature.
(*the editor requires python3 / python3-tk)
[/LEFT]
[/CENTER]

---

:confused:

:idea: If the dash presented these options alongside the existing icons_with text_ as well as the icon it would improve the functionality & be easier for a new user to understand - after all there is plenty of room along the bottom & make the 'help' easier to find would also be of assistance - one of Ubuntus selling points is that the help actually does that (unlike windows) which means it mustn't auto hide on the top bar - stick it up the top near the mail icon or somewhere like that where it can be accessed easily.

### Post by Jacobonbuntu on 2012-06-16
> **nll said:**
> @Jacobonbuntu: My feedback to you would be that I like your program's functionality, but I don't like that it does not have a native look. In addition, we now have lots of different programs to customize Unity, so it would be better if it got integrated in one of them.

Thanks!
about your second remark: in my opinion there is a difference between settings that you make one time (or in any case not regularly), like the size of the screen fonts and other desktop settings, and settings that are constantly "under construction" and frequently edited, like the organization of the quicklists.
For the first category I would prefer one general settings tool, like My Unity, Ubuntu Tweak etc. To the second category I would like to see a "straight road"; a one click access to a tool with advanced options.  integration in another program would be distracting to me.

### Post by Randy M on 2012-06-19
A few thoughts from a "N00B" on Unity. I'm not a power user, I don't do command line unless I'm forced into it. I started with Ubuntu 8.something and have found it to be a good solid system. 12.04 and Unity both seem solid, but I do have a few things that bother me.


[LIST=1]
[*]The launcher gets too crowded. Are there any plans to allow for "grouping" the apps? It would be nice to put the audio apps in one, video apps in another, and so on.
[*]Put a running apps section in the dash. I know they're tagged with little arrows, but a quick list somewhere to select what's open would make things easier
[*]Let me move the launcher around. I've seen some ways to put it on the bottom, but an approved and supported method would help.
[*]An approved and supported way to make desktop launchers would also be nice. I found a way to create my own, but a quick way would help. There are still a few apps out there that don't create launchers on their own. I'm still trying to make a desktop launcher to make desktop launchers, but no luck yet.
[*]Themes. I'd like a few more choices. Mainly, I'd like the Minimize, Maximize, and Close buttons on the right instead of the left. I work on a Windows system (office property, not under my control) all day and switching at the end of the day takes a little time.
[/LIST]
None of these are killers. Unity takes a little getting used to, but there was an adjustment when I went from Windows 1 to Windows 2 to Windows 3 to Windows 95 and so on. I invite those complaining about Unity to take a look at the Windows 8 screenshots. I shudder to think of the day when my office makes that switch.

---

### Post by deadflowr on 2012-09-30

Unknown errors are hard to determine if they have fixes.
I would suggest, try running in 3d, to see if the error still occurs. If it doesn't good, if it does, the error should run the error report program apport(as long as you didn't disable it). Either report the error to launchpad, or look for the error report in /var/crash, and post a new thread in the forums.

### Post by mbuell on 2012-12-21
> **mbuell said:**
> I'd like also to propose some changes to Unity.

1. Make it possible to CHOOSE which "lens" is default.
2. Give us a "lens" that will reasonably approximate the Gnome2/xfce/Windows/kde menuing systems. MENUS WORK! 

Btw, I get it with why you chose to break free of the Gnome direction - I get that. I get it that you want to choose a path and stick to it. But what I don't get is why you are taking something that ain't broken, and trying to "fix" it. That is tinkering for the sake of it, and it will only bring you heartache in the market. The market is already telling you that - you've seen the numbers. 

Good luck, and please give us back a direction for Ubuntu that looks like you intend to stay mainstream.

BTW, I know we are not supposed to bash Unity. I'm trying not to - so my productive suggestion is to do for Unity what I am told Cinammon does for Gnome3 - tries to bridge the gap to allow the new paradigm while maintaining a working methodology. Menus became dominant because they work. Don't force users to "find a tweak" to make this happen with the "lenses" - because then Grandma won't do it. She's at the toaster level, you know. Finding a tweak is one step too far for her. So make it an obvious choice. My $.02.

---
